How To Build A Company With AI From The Ground Up

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·10:28 ·12.3K views · 2026-04-24
Diana Hu (Partner, Y Combinator)
AI is evolving from a productivity tool into the fundamental operating system of the startup, enabling "closed-loop" organizations that replace human middle management with a queryable intelligence layer.
TL;DR AI is evolving from a productivity tool into the fundamental operating system of the startup, enabling "closed-loop" organizations that replace human middle management with a queryable intelligence layer. By prioritizing "tokenmaxxing" (API spend) over headcount, AI-native...

TL;DR

AI is evolving from a productivity tool into the fundamental operating system of the startup, enabling "closed-loop" organizations that replace human middle management with a queryable intelligence layer. By prioritizing "tokenmaxxing" (API spend) over headcount, AI-native founders can achieve 1,000x output compared to traditional competitors.

AI as the Company Operating System

  • From Productivity to Capability: AI shouldn't be framed as a mere "copilot" for existing workflows. Instead, it represents a shift toward entirely new capabilities where a single person can build features that previously required an entire team.
  • The Intelligence Layer: Founders should treat AI as the central operating system. Every decision, workflow, and process must flow through an intelligent layer that constantly learns and self-improves.
  • Closed-Loop Systems: Unlike traditional "open-loop" companies—where decisions are made without systematic feedback—AI-native companies run as closed loops. These systems continuously monitor outputs and adjust internal processes to meet stated goals with higher correctness and stability.

Creating a Queryable, Artifact-Rich Organization

  • Total Legibility: To function as a closed loop, the entire organization must be "queryable" or legible to AI. This requires capturing every important action as a digital artifact.
      • Tactical Implementation: Use AI note-takers for all meetings, minimize private DMs and emails in favor of public channels, and embed agents across all communication streams.
  • Automated Management: When a company is queryable, agents can replace manual coordination.
      • Engineering Sprints: AI agents with access to tickets (Linear), Slack, customer feedback (Pylon), and GitHub can analyze past performance and propose future plans more accurately than human managers.
      • Performance Gains: Teams adopting these practices have reported cutting engineering sprint times in half while achieving 10x the output.

The AI Software Factory and the 1,000x Engineer

  • Evolution of Development: The highest-velocity companies are moving toward "AI Software Factories," an evolution of Test-Driven Development (TDD).
      • Human Role: Humans define the spec and the success tests.
      • AI Role: Agents generate the implementation and iterate until the tests pass.
  • Removing Handwritten Code: Some advanced teams, such as the AI group at StrongDM, have reached a point where repositories contain zero handwritten code—only specs and test harnesses.
  • The 1,000x Engineer: By surrounding a single engineer with a system of agents, founders can finally realize the "1,000x engineer" (a concept popularized by Steve Yegge), enabling individuals to build products that were previously impossible.

Structural Shifts: Eliminating Human Middleware

  • Dissolving Hierarchies: Traditional middle management exists to route information between layers. In an AI-native company, the intelligence layer handles this routing, making human middleware unnecessary and eliminating information loss.
  • The Three New Archetypes: Following the model discussed by Jack Dorsey (Block), AI-native companies will consist of three specific roles:

1. Individual Contributors (ICs): Builders across all functions (Eng, Ops, Support) who use prototypes instead of pitch decks.

2. Directly Responsible Individuals (DRIs): Leaders focused solely on strategy and customer outcomes rather than people management.

3. AI Founders: Leaders who build and lead by example, refusing to delegate their AI strategy to subordinates.

  • Tokenmaxxing vs. Headcount: Success will be measured by maximizing token usage rather than hiring. Founders should be comfortable with "uncomfortably high" API bills because they replace the far higher costs of an inflated headcount.

The Startup Advantage

  • The Legacy Debt Trap: Incumbents face "standard operating procedures" and entrenched hierarchies that are difficult to unwind. Every attempt to go AI-native risks breaking a working legacy product.
  • The Clean Slate: Early-stage founders have a massive edge because they can build queryable systems and agent-driven workflows from day one without the need for retraining or reorganization.
  • Developing Conviction: Founders cannot outsource their AI strategy. Diana Hu emphasizes that founders must personally spend time with coding agents and tools to break their own priors about what is possible.
"The way to think about AI is that it should not be a tool your company just uses. It should be the operating system your company runs on." — Diana Hu
"Maximizing token usage, not headcount, will be the critical shift. The best companies will be the ones that are tokenmaxxing." — Diana Hu
"The company itself has to be rebuilt as an intelligence layer, with humans at the edge guiding it rather than routing information through it." — Diana Hu (referencing Jack Dorsey)
Hi, I'm Diana, and I'm a partner at YC. Over the past few months, it's become clear to me that AI is not just going to change how quickly software gets built or what workflows get automated. It's going to fundamentally change the way startups should be run, from what roles will exist to what products are possible to build. In this episode, I'm going to discuss how founders should think about building an AI native company, what roles their team should have, and what concrete internal practices they can adopt right now to move much faster. Currently, most people talk about AI in terms of productivity. They'll talk at length about how it can make engineers more productive, or say we need to add copilots to existing workflows and ship more features. This framing misses the shift we're currently seeing, which is less about productivity boosts than entirely new capabilities. The right person with AI tools can now build features that used to require an entire team or were just impossible. Thinking about AI in terms of new capabilities has several implications for how founders should run their companies. At a high level, the way to think about AI is that it should not be a tool your company just uses. It should be the operating system your company runs on. Every workflow, every decision, and every process should flow through an intelligent layer that is constantly learning and improving. What this means concretely is every important process in your company should be captured by an intelligent closed loop. A closed loop captures information, feeds it back into an intelligent system, and improves the process over time. If you've ever studied control systems, you'll be familiar with the difference between an open-loop and a closed-loop system. Open loops are control systems without feedback loops. In the old world, companies basically ran as open loops. You made a decision, executed it, and didn't always systematically measure the outcome and adjust the process. Open loops are inherently lossy. A closed loop, on the other hand, is self-regulating. It continuously monitors its output and adjusts its process to better meet the stated goal. Closed loops are extremely powerful for correctness and stability. With self-improving agents, your company should run as a closed loop. To build these closed loops, you will need to make your entire company queryable. In other words, the whole organization should be legible to AI. Every important action should produce an artifact that the intelligence at the center of the company can learn from and use to self-improve. This means recording your meetings with an AI note taker, minimizing DMs and emails, and embedding agents throughout communication of all channels. It also means building custom dashboards with everything in the company: revenue, sales, engineering, hiring, ops, everything. Here's a concrete example of how it could work. Take engineering management and sprint planning. If you have an agent that has access to your Linear tickets, all your Slack engineering channels, all customer feedback from emails or tools like Pylon and GitHub, high-level plans in a Notion or Google Doc, sales calls and recordings from daily standups, then the agent can analyze what was actually shipped in your previous sprint and how well they met customers' needs for real. From there, you can go a step further. With full visibility into what shipped, what worked, and what didn't, agents can start looking ahead. They can propose sprint plans for engineers that are way more predictable and accurate and on track. The days of eng manager status rollups that are super lossy are gone. Having managed engineering teams myself, and now seeing this across multiple YC companies, this is a game changer. What used to require constant coordination becomes legible and queryable by default. I've seen teams that do this cut their engineering sprint time in half and get close to 10x more done in that time. The overarching principle here is that to get their full capabilities, you need to provide models with as much context as you would provide an employee. When you do this, your company stops operating as an open loop, where information is fragmented and manually interpreted. It becomes instead a closed-loop system. Status, decisions, and outcomes are continuously captured and fed back into this intelligence layer. The result is a system that always has an up-to-date view of what's actually happening. There's also a new paradigm emerging for how the highest velocity companies build product: AI software factories. If you're familiar with test-driven development or TDD, this is the next evolution of that. With software factories, humans write a spec and a set of tests that define success, and then AI agents generate the implementation and code and iterate until the tests pass. The human defines what to build and judges the output. The actual code is the agent's job. Some companies have already pushed this to the point where their repos contain no handwritten code, just specs and test harnesses. StrongDM's AI team is an example of how to do this. Their end goal was a system that essentially eliminated the need for a human to write or review code, and so they built their own software factory where specs and scenario-based validations drive agents to write, test, and iterate on code until it meets a probabilistic satisfaction threshold. And it works. This is how you achieve the thousand-X engineer that Steve Yegge talks about by surrounding a single engineer with a system of agents that enable them to build things they would have never been able to build before. The era of the thousand or even 10,000 X engineer is here. One implication of building your company this way with AI loops everywhere, a queryable organization, and software factories, is that the classic management hierarchy no longer makes sense. In the old world, you needed middle managers and coordinators to route information inefficiently up and down an organization. In the new world, the intelligence layer serves that purpose. If your company is queryable, artifact rich, and legible to an AI, you should have almost no human middleware. This matters because your company's velocity is only as fast as its information flow. Every layer of human routing you can remove is a direct speed gain. A great example is what Jack Dorsey is doing over at Block. After going deep on the tools, he's come to the same conclusion many have. This is about more than just incremental productivity gains. His view is that if you keep the same org chart and management structure, you miss the shift entirely. The company itself has to be rebuilt as an intelligence layer, with humans at the edge guiding it rather than routing information through it. Going forward, Jack suggests every company will have three employee archetypes. The first is the individual contributor or IC. Basically the builder operator. This is someone who directly makes and runs things. In an AI native company, this is not limited to engineers. Everyone builds: eng, ops, support, sales. Everyone comes to meetings with working prototypes, not pitch decks. Second is the DRI, the directly responsible individual, focused on strategy and customer outcomes. This is not a classic manager. It's the person with a clear responsibility for the result. One person, one outcome, no hiding. The third is the AI founder type. This person still builds, still coaches, and leads by example. If you're the founder, this needs to be you at the forefront, showing your team what massive capability gains look like, not delegating your AI strategy to someone else. With this structure, companies will be able to get outsized results with much smaller teams. Maximizing token usage, not headcount, will be the critical shift. The best companies will be the ones that are tokenmaxxing. Think of the tradeoff this way. One person with AI tools can be the equivalent of what used to take a large engineering team at a pre-AI company. That means dramatically leaner engineering, design, HR, and admin teams. And so you should be willing to run an uncomfortably high API bill, because it's replacing what would have taken a far more expensive and inflated headcount. But don't just take my word for any of this. You cannot outsource your conviction on the power of these tools. You need to develop it yourself by actually sitting with coding agents and using them until you start to break your own priors about what is now possible to build. If you are an early stage founder, you have a huge advantage in getting ahead on this. You don't have legacy systems, entrenched org charts, or thousands of people to retrain. You are small enough to build your company right from day one. The opposite is the case for existing companies. They have to maintain and grow a live product while unwinding years of standard operating procedures and core assumptions about how software gets built. Some companies can achieve this by spinning up small internal skunkwork teams that can build AI native systems from scratch, separate from the core business. Mutiny is a great example of this. But for most, every change to their core processes risks breaking something that already works. So by their nature, these large companies will have a much harder time going AI native. Startups don't have that constraint, and that's a major edge to take advantage of. You can design your systems, workflows, and culture around AI from the start, and as a result, operate a thousand times faster than the incumbent.

Meet the New Thiel Fellows

John Coogan(455.0K) ·135:34 ·967 views · 2026-04-24
John Coogan (Host) Peter Thiel (Founder, Thiel Fellowship)
The latest Thiel Fellowship class is leveraging AI and custom hardware to disrupt legacy industries—ranging from autonomous logistics and small-cap equity research to government fraud detection—amidst the release of GPT 5.5 and rising national securi...
TL;DR The latest Thiel Fellowship class is leveraging AI and custom hardware to disrupt legacy industries—ranging from autonomous logistics and small-cap equity research to government fraud detection—amidst the release of GPT 5.5 and rising national security concerns regarding...

TL;DR

The latest Thiel Fellowship class is leveraging AI and custom hardware to disrupt legacy industries—ranging from autonomous logistics and small-cap equity research to government fraud detection—amidst the release of GPT 5.5 and rising national security concerns regarding AI model "distillation" by foreign entities.

The New Thiel Fellowship Class: Industry Disruptors

The fellowship continues to provide $250,000 grants to young entrepreneurs to build companies in lieu of attending college.

  • Logistics & Robotics:
      • Victor Boyd (Cavalla): Building autonomous forklifts from the ground up.
          • Hardware Strategy: Moved away from retrofitting existing machinery (the "comma.ai approach") to building custom hardware (the "Tesla approach") for better reliability.
          • Target Market: Currently deployed in pharmaceutical warehouses where pallet damage can cost hundreds of thousands of dollars.
      • Milan (Opt 32): Developing full-stack compute infrastructure for physical autonomy.
          • Full-Stack Scope: Building everything from compilers to custom chips (ASICs) to run on-device machine learning for robots and drones.
  • Information & Investigation:
      • Alex (The Antifraud Company): Utilizing AI for investigative journalism and legal action against government fraudsters.
          • Business Model: Operates on contingency, keeping 15–30% of recovered funds under whistleblower laws.
          • Scope: Monitoring $600 billion in annual government fraud across healthcare, defense, and SBA loans.
      • Nick (Everyticker): The youngest-ever Thiel Fellow, building an AI-powered equity research platform.
          • Market Gap: Provides high-quality research on 5,000+ small and micro-cap stocks that Wall Street analysts typically ignore.
  • Platform & Recruitment Intelligence:
      • Ishan Gupta (Juicebox): An AI recruiting agent that moves beyond keyword pattern matching.
          • Talent Analysis: Analyzes real work products (e.g., GitHub contributions) to find the best hires for companies like Ramp and Scale.
      • Galen Mead (Standard Intelligence): Developing "computer-use" models.
          • AI Agents: Training base models to interact with computers via screen and keyboard as a universal form factor for AGI.

Emerging Global Super-Apps and Infrastructure

  • Aubrey (Swoop): Building a "Super App" for Africa, starting with food delivery in Nigeria.
      • Financial Strategy: Uses food delivery as a low-cost acquisition wedge to convert users to high-margin peer-to-peer payment services.
  • Samuel (Praso): Building the digital infrastructure for wholesale commerce in Brazil.
      • SMB Focus: Digitizing procurement and credit for 10,000+ restaurants, providing a one-stop-shop that manages logistics and short-term (10-day) credit.
  • Claire Wang: Conducting biologically accurate simulations of nervous systems.
      • Research Goal: Simulating the *C. elegans* (worm) brain to better understand consciousness and improve brain-computer interfaces (BCI).

AI Frontier: GPT 5.5 and National Security

The tech landscape is shifting rapidly with new model releases and increasing regulatory scrutiny.

  • GPT 5.5 Release: OpenAI has launched GPT 5.5, designed specifically for "real work" and powering agents.
      • Capabilities: Excels at using tools, checking its own work, and completing complex multi-step tasks across software environments.
  • Adversarial Distillation: Michael Kratsios (former U.S. CTO) issued a warning regarding foreign entities, specifically China, using "industrial-scale" campaigns to distill American frontier models.
      • The Threat: Using tens of thousands of proxy accounts to extract model capabilities, effectively stealing billions in R&D value at the API level.
  • Industry "Slop": Discussion of "Choppelgangers"—a new slang term for lower-quality, distilled versions of frontier models, often from Chinese open-source labs.

Market Intelligence & Corporate Restructuring

  • Medallia Writedown: Thoma Bravo is reportedly handing the software firm Medallia over to creditors, resulting in a $5.1 billion equity wipeout.
      • The Cause: High debt loads ($3B) and concerns that AI will supplant its customer feedback services.
  • Intel Earnings: Intel stock rose 15% after-hours following a Q1 beat and strong guidance driven by data center sales.
  • Meta & Microsoft: Both firms are leaning out, with Meta cutting 10% of its workforce (8,000 employees) and Microsoft offering early retirement to approximately 7% of its staff.
  • Bob Iger: Reported to be joining Thrive Capital following his tenure at Disney.
"The customer does not care if you’re fully autonomous. Customers care if the work is done... If you’re getting the job done every single time and you’re profitable, the customer could not care less." — Victor Boyd, CEO of Cavalla
"Wall Street only covers the mega and large-cap stocks. We use LLMs to generate high-quality research on every single U.S. stock, including the ones that Wall Street doesn’t cover." — Nick, Founder of Everyticker
"There is a big anti-fraud moment in the United States... $600 billion a year is being stolen by people contracting with the government. We need to restore trust in our institutions." — Alex, Co-Founder of The Antifraud Company

And if you puzzle piece those together, all of a sudden you're getting a really solid map of what a frontier system is capable of. And so it feels like we, the industry, are the victim of a distillation attack even if it might not register among a single lab because that single lab is only getting hit with like one-third or one-fifth of the total attack. Good news. We can pull up this post from Kratsios, I'll read the letter. Subject: Adversarial Distillation of American AI Models. The United States leads the world in AI technologies. That lead reflects decades of foundational research, bold entrepreneurial risk-taking and hundreds of billions of dollars in annual private investment. American AI leadership drives economic growth, strengthens national security and advances the frontiers of science, medicine and human knowledge. The breakthroughs emerging from American industry raise living standards, expand opportunity and improve lives around the world. There's five blind men and they're each touching a different part of the elephant. The guy who's touching the legs thinks it's a tree, the guy who's touching the trunk thinks it's a rope. Fraud is kind of like this, it always leaves traces, but it's somewhat difficult to put them back together. Help me bridge the gap between what we've seen in BCI with mostly reading from the brain getting an X and Y output so you can control a mouse on a computer—incredibly impactful technology—and then simulating nervous systems. How are these two things related in your mind? Why are they important to overlap? Yeah, yeah. So I think right now BCI technology is very powerful in the sense that it is on a great path towards clinical applications. Soon, people who are paraplegic can maybe walk again and people who are blind can see again. But a lot of this comes from almost like post-hoc problem solving. You throw in enough data at a model of someone's brain and maybe you can help them move their arm in the XY direction or move a mouse in a specific way. But when you are able to actually decode information from the brain and read and understand exact signals coming from every region of the brain, being able to truly control the brain—for example, if you know which regions to activate—then you have a more naturalistic control method. So like instead of only being able to move my arm left or right, you can move your arm very naturalistically in any degree of freedom. And I think that's kind of the power of being able to simulate the brain. You could do a lot of research to understand one of the most complex things in the universe. And I mean, obviously, we're not starting with the human brain, but any level tells you the sort of data that you need. Like, do I need voltage data or is calcium data enough? Is light sheet or electron microscopy enough? So there's all these questions the C. elegans can answer, and once we answer that question, obviously the goal is to move on into zebrafish and mice and fly and so on. But right now we are not close to mouse, for example, because there's no way to image the mouse brain while the mouse is still alive. But you can with C. elegans. Fascinating. Defense Secretary and Joint Chiefs Chair News Conference

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·32:05 ·2.5K views · 2026-04-24
Pete Hegseth (Defense Secretary)
Operation Epic Fury has established a decisive global maritime blockade against Iran, successfully turning back 34 vessels and seizing several "dark fleet" ships to ensure the regime never acquires a nuclear weapon.
TL;DR Operation Epic Fury has established a decisive global maritime blockade against Iran, successfully turning back 34 vessels and seizing several "dark fleet" ships to ensure the regime never acquires a nuclear weapon. The Department of War has authorized a "shoot-to-kill"...

TL;DR

Operation Epic Fury has established a decisive global maritime blockade against Iran, successfully turning back 34 vessels and seizing several "dark fleet" ships to ensure the regime never acquires a nuclear weapon. The Department of War has authorized a "shoot-to-kill" policy for any Iranian fast boats attempting to mine the Strait of Hormuz or disrupt global shipping.

Current Military Operations & Blockade Status

  • The Iranian Blockade: Secretary Hegseth confirmed an "iron-clad" blockade is in effect from the Gulf of Oman to the open oceans.
      • Enforcement Success: As of the briefing, 34 vessels have been turned around after meeting the blockade criteria.
      • Strategic Control: The U.S. Navy currently controls all transit through the Strait of Hormuz, maintaining a "nothing in, nothing out" policy for Iranian ports.
  • Naval Reinforcements: A second aircraft carrier is scheduled to join the blockade within days to bolster presence and deterrence.
  • Counter-Piracy Logic: The administration views the IRGC not as a professional military, but as "criminals on the high seas" and "pirates with a flag" who target random commercial vessels.

Global Interdictions & Seizures

  • The Tusca Incident: The Joint Chiefs Chair detailed the seizure of the *MV Tusca*, a 965-foot container ship that attempted to breach the blockade.
      • Escalation Ladder: After the crew ignored five warning shots over six hours, the U.S. Navy fired nine inert rounds into the engine room to disable the vessel.
      • Marine Intervention: U.S. Marines subsequently boarded the ship via helicopter fast-roping; the vessel and crew remain in U.S. custody.
  • Indo-Pacific Operations: The blockade has "gone global," targeting sanctioned Iranian "dark fleet" ships far beyond the Middle East.
      • MV Tiffany: A Very Large Crude Tanker (VLCC) carrying approximately 2 million barrels of sanctioned oil was seized by INDOPACOM forces.
      • Majestic X: A stateless tanker was interdicted in the Indian Ocean on April 22 and remains in U.S. custody.

Rules of Engagement & Mine Threats

  • Lethal Authority: President Trump has authorized the U.S. Navy to "shoot and kill" to destroy any Iranian fast boats attempting to lay mines or threaten American forces.
  • Clear Directives: Commanders have been issued rules of engagement similar to those used against drug-running boats in the Caribbean—using immediate force to neutralize threats to commercial shipping.
  • The Mine Menace: While transit is occurring through specific open paths, the Secretary characterized Iranian mine-laying as a direct violation of the current ceasefire.

Strategic Shifts & Allied Expectations

  • The "Department of War": Hegseth emphasized the symbolic and functional shift from "Defense" to the "War Department," focused on being proactive rather than reactive.
  • Ending "Free Riding": The Secretary issued a sharp critique of European and Asian allies, noting that while the U.S. is energy independent, Europe relies heavily on the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Demand for Action: Hegseth stated the time for "fancy conferences" is over and allies need to deploy their own naval assets to protect their energy interests.
  • Afghanistan Review: The Department is conducting a comprehensive review of the 2021 Afghanistan withdrawal to establish accountability and ensure proper recognition for heroes at Abbey Gate.

Notable Quotes

"Iran's battered military, the IRGC specifically, has been reduced to a gang of pirates with a flag. They cloak their aggression in slogans, but the world now sees them for what they are: criminals on the high seas." — Secretary Hegseth
"We are enforcing the blockade across the board against any ship of any nationality that is transiting to or from an Iranian port or territory... As of this morning, 34 ships have met the U.S. blockade and made the wise choice to turn around." — Joint Chiefs Chair
"The time for free riding is over. America and the free world deserve allies who are capable, who are loyal, and who understand that being an ally is not a one-way street." — Secretary Hegseth
"If Iran is putting mines in the water or otherwise threatening American commercial shipping or American forces, we will shoot to destroy. No hesitation." — Secretary Hegseth

Mr. Chairman, over to you. Thank you, Mr. Secretary, and good morning everyone. Thanks for being here. Today marks 55 days since the President of the United States ordered major combat operations against Iran. Today and this morning the joint force remains postured and prepared to recommence major combat operations on the order of the President. On April 8th the President announced a ceasefire and last week at the President's direction and on order of the Secretary, US CENTCOM began enforcing a blockade against all commercial vessels going into and out of Iran and their ports. Simultaneously, the US military, in support of the US Department of Justice, was ordered to conduct maritime interdiction operations against sanctioned and dark fleet vessels globally. This operation also began on April 8th and what I'd like to do this morning is update all of you on those actions and activities. First, as I mentioned, US Central Command continues to maintain a strict blockade on all the ports. We're enforcing the blockade across the board against any ship of any nationality that is transiting to or from an Iranian port or territory. We're closely tracking vessels of interest headed towards Iran and those moving away from Iran that were outside the blockade area when this blockade was ordered. And as everyone saw this past weekend, we're prepared and postured to intercept them. As of this morning, 34 ships have met the US blockade and made the wise choice to turn around. One ship did not, and that ship was the motor vessel Tusca. Over several hours this past Sunday, April 19th, the US conducted maritime interdiction operations against the Tusca whose crew attempted to breach the blockade line. The Tusca is a large container ship, about 965 feet long or about the same size as a United States Navy aircraft carrier and over 100 feet wide. The operation began about 4 a.m. Eastern Standard Time when the United States Navy destroyer approached her and the crew and the vessel ignored warnings. And I've described how the destroyer force is operating in the in the area prior. Upon intercept and coming within visual range, American forces began to issue several clear and unambiguous warnings and informed the vessel and their crew that they were in a violation of the US blockade and directed the ship to turn around. Over a six-hour period, the vessel and her crew repeatedly ignored US warnings. And then the US Navy destroyer executed a series of pre-planned, carefully calibrated escalation options, including firing five warning shots. The vessel and her crew continued to ignore warnings. And after exhausting all other measures, CENTCOM authorized disabling fire against the Tusca. US sailors warned the crew of the Tusca to abandon the engine room. At approximately 9 a.m. Eastern Standard Time, the destroyer disabled the Tusca's engine by firing nine inert rounds from the destroyer's Mark 45 5-inch guns precisely into the engine room and engine space on board the Tusca. Not surprisingly, the vessel then reported issues with their engine, went dead in the water and began to comply with US directions and orders. At 4 p.m. Eastern Standard Time, CENTCOM ordered United States Marines to seize the ship. Via helicopters, US Marines maneuvered quickly to the disabled ship, boarded her from fast ropes via helicopter infiltration methods and took custody of the ship. The ship and her crew remain safe in US custody today. Let me now turn to global operations in the Pacific and I want to highlight that this is a major interagency effort between the United States military, the White House, the Department of Homeland Security, and the Department of Justice. On Monday, April 20th, US INDOPACOM forces in support of a Department of Justice request interdicted the motor tanker Tiffany which was transporting sanctioned Iranian oil. The Tiffany is a very large crude tanker, VLCC for short, and is also about the size of a US aircraft carrier and capable of transporting approximately 2 million barrels of oil. At approximately 11 p.m. Eastern Standard Time, US military forces and law enforcement forces also went to the ship via rotary-wing platforms, fast-roped onto her deck and secured her. After the ship was secured, a US Navy control team embarked aboard the Tiffany and assumed control of the ship at 4 a.m. Eastern Standard Time on April 21st. A Navy control team is comprised of sailors led by a boarding officer that assumes control of a seized ship in order to secure the crew, the cargo and the vessel. And disposition of that first vessel is still pending. The next interdiction occurred on Wednesday, this past Wednesday, 22 April, when INDOPACOM conducted yet another interdiction again at the request of the US Department of Justice. This time the stateless motor tanker Majestic X, also known as the Ponix, also in the Indian Ocean. This is again a VLCC or very large crude tanker. At approximately 11 p.m. Eastern Standard Time, US forces began the interdiction again using rotary-wing assets to get to the ship, got on board and secured the bridge. Both ships, the Tiffany, the Majestic X, and their crews remain in US custody. And we will continue to conduct similar maritime interdiction actions and activities in the Pacific and Indian Ocean against Iranian ships and vessels of the dark fleet. The actions by our service members and our law enforcement partners demonstrate the incredible discipline and dedication and professionalism of our joint force. Intel Delivers Strong AI-Fueled Outlook | Bloomberg Tech

Bloomberg Podcasts(472.0K) ·44:36 ·37 views · 2026-04-24
Lip Bu Tan (CEO, Intel) Ed Ludlow (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
Intel has reached a historic stock record by surpassing its 2000-era peak, driven by a resurgence in CPU demand for AI servers, while Big Tech giants Meta and Microsoft are executing massive layoffs to offset surging AI infrastructure spending.
TL;DR Intel has reached a historic stock record by surpassing its 2000-era peak, driven by a resurgence in CPU demand for AI servers, while Big Tech giants Meta and Microsoft are executing massive layoffs to offset surging AI infrastructure spending. Market Milestone: Intel...

TL;DR

Intel has reached a historic stock record by surpassing its 2000-era peak, driven by a resurgence in CPU demand for AI servers, while Big Tech giants Meta and Microsoft are executing massive layoffs to offset surging AI infrastructure spending.

Intel’s "Glory Days" Return

  • Market Milestone: Intel shares surged to a record high, surpassing their dot-com era peak with a year-to-date gain of over 120% in 2026.
  • CPU Resurgence: Bloomberg’s Ian King notes that general-purpose CPUs, once considered "backup singers" to Nvidia's accelerators, are now essential for AI servers.
      • Supply Constraints: CEO Pat Gelsinger reported that demand for CPUs currently exceeds Intel's ability to supply.
  • Foundry Business Progress:
      • Process Milestones: Intel is seeing 6–7x production increases in its 18A process.
      • Strategic Position: Benchmark analyst Cody Acree raised his price target to $105, arguing Intel is the only viable US-based alternative to TSMC.
      • Advanced Packaging: CFO David Zinsner highlighted that packaging has grown from a "hundreds of millions" opportunity to a multi-billion dollar business.

The Human Cost of the AI Pivot

  • Massive Job Cuts: Meta and Microsoft are cutting or buying out approximately 23,000 roles combined.
      • Meta: Planning to trim 10% of its workforce (8,000 layoffs and 6,000 unfilled roles) to offset a projected $135 billion in capital expenditures (CAPEX).
      • Microsoft: Offering voluntary buyouts for the first time, potentially reducing its US workforce by 7% as data center costs mount.
  • Margin Maintenance: Analysts suggest these cuts are designed to keep margins flat rather than boost them, as companies shift from high-margin software sales to lower-margin GPU infrastructure.
  • Sector-Wide Trend: Oracle also recently cut thousands of jobs, signaling an industry-wide "milking" of legacy cash-cow businesses to fund AI build-outs.

AI Infrastructure: Energy and Defense

  • X Energy IPO: The nuclear energy firm, backed by Amazon, launched its IPO at $23/share (opening near $28).
      • Data Center Power: CEO Clay Sell emphasized that safe, "meltdown-proof" small modular reactors (SMRs) are the key to making nuclear energy "economic, boring, and financeable" for the AI era.
  • Defense Innovation: Andreessen Horowitz (a16z) is backing UDC, a startup building autonomous underwater vehicles.
      • Strategic Importance: General Partner Aaron Price Wright highlighted the need to protect undersea fiber cables and maritime interests in conflict zones like the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Cost Disruption: UDC aims to produce units for $40,000–$60,000, compared to legacy defense systems costing $2 million–$5 million.

Looking Ahead: The $15 Trillion Earnings Week

  • Breaking News: Google is committing up to $40 billion in investment to AI lab Anthropic, starting with $10 billion in cash at a $350 billion valuation.
  • Crucial Calendar: Next week, tech giants representing $15 trillion in market cap—including Microsoft, Alphabet, Amazon, Meta, and Apple—will report earnings.
  • Investor Focus: Market attention is shifting from simple "AI hype" to a strict demand for Return on Investment (ROI) and clarity on future CAPEX spending.
"The CPU is hot again—this general-purpose product that was the backup singer to the AI accelerators from Nvidia is suddenly taking center stage."

— Ian King, Bloomberg Semiconductor Reporter

"Intel really is the only alternative to Taiwan semi [TSMC], and to bring that back into the US is a real strategic necessity for US manufacturing."

— Cody Acree, Equity Research Analyst at Benchmark

"Our submarine capabilities haven't really advanced in half a century. It costs billions of dollars and puts lives at risk... many of the things we need to worry about in the ocean today actually don't need manned crews."

— Aaron Price Wright, General Partner at Andreessen Horowitz

Coming up, Intel crushes earnings, the stock blasts through its .com era peak, and a phone call with CEO Pat Gelsinger. Plus tech layoffs ramp up, with Microsoft and Meta making thousands of job cuts as AI spending surges, and OpenAI comes out with a new model that is better at helping scientists and carrying out more complex tasks. Intel shares have blown through their .com peak to a stunning new record high. Strong earnings and outlook, yes, but this has been an unstoppable train all year. Intel is up more than 120% so far in 2026. That is the story: Intel is guiding well ahead of the street at 13.8 to 14.8 billion in revenues for the current quarter, compared to a consensus of 13 billion. Is this Intel back to its glory days? Has Pat Gelsinger turned this company around? Bloomberg's Ian King has led semiconductor coverage here since 1998. It's all about the CPU. They're in the right place at the right time in a world where AI servers need CPUs. Is that the story? "Yeah, I mean, as we've said previously, the CPU is hot again," King says. This general-purpose product, which was kind of the backup singer to the AI accelerators from Nvidia, is suddenly taking center stage again. Obviously, Intel is the biggest producer of this. Do we know if Intel is back or not? We'll see once the market slows down and we'll see how competitive it is, but right now everybody needs a CPU. So Ian, you and I spoke to Pat Gelsinger on the phone, and to summarize, the situation with CPU is that demand is greater than their ability to supply. The street would say that's a good problem to have. But why is there this severe reaction in the stock? You know the history of this company inside and out. If you look at other metrics, we're nowhere near the heady days of 60% margin. Try to explain what the confidence is here. Yeah, I mean, there are a couple of things. Obviously, the market tries to anticipate where a company will be in, say, six months' time, so there's an element of that going on. But part of this was that, look, there was a lot of money put into Intel and there was a lot of hope going in there, and we've seen that kind of fall short before. This time, they delivered. This time, the numbers were good—as good as or if not better than expectations—so there's kind of a bit of a relief rally going on here. Let's take Intel seriously again, but as we heard from the CEO himself—Ed, you'll remember this—he's like, "Look, we're not all the way there yet. We've still got a lot of work to do." And that was what he said a lot. There was an element of modesty and caution that Pat Gelsinger certainly put across to us. David Zinsner, the CFO, was also on the call. And actually, if there's an area where they're cautioning, it's in that client category, PC. Explain what Zinsner said and also talk foundry there, too. Yeah, I mean, this is a general phenomenon, right? There's just not enough memory chips in the world, and guess what? The memory that is available is going to go to the most expensive, the most profitable systems, which right now is the data center. So that's bad for the PC. Second half of the year, PC is not going to be as strong. On the flip side of things, Intel's foundry business, which is the business of making chips, is still losing a lot of money. It still has a lot of work to do to get to the level that it needs to be. We need to see those big external customers get announced or start to appear in the second half of the year for that part of the business, and it's a very expensive part of the business to really have turned the corner. I wasn't doing this in the year 2000 when Intel was at its last peak; you were, and you've led the way on covering this company ever since. Bloomberg's Ian King, thank you very much. The Justice Department is ending a controversial investigation into building renovation cost overruns by the Federal Reserve, potentially clearing a path to confirmation for Kevin Warsh, President Donald Trump's pick to be the next Fed leader. Bloomberg's Katie Linds and Joe Matthew, the Balance of Power team, are standing by with more. And thank you so much. Here with us in our Washington, D.C. studio is the Chair of the House Financial Services Committee, Congressman French Hill of Arkansas. Thank you so much for being here, Mr. Chairman. As we get this news that the probe—the criminal probe at least—has been dropped by the DOJ, the IG at the Fed is now going to be taking this over. Is this the best possible outcome and should we consider this now done and dusted? "Well, I think it's a good outcome. It's something that I've felt should be done for a number of weeks," says Hill. I said back on January 12th that I felt like the accusation that Chairman Powell had committed some sort of crime connected with the building construction or his testimony about it was a distraction. It would delay President Trump in the selection of a high-quality new chair for the Fed and the important economic policy that should be coordinated between the Fed, the US Treasury, and the legislative branch. So it's a step in the right direction; I applaud it. I hope it comes to pass, and I hope that then facilitates the prompt confirmation of Kevin Warsh. Mr. Chairman, J. Powell made it clear that he was going to hang around as long as this investigation was underway. Now that it appears to be ending, do you expect that he will plan to leave the Fed? Well, that's a decision Jay has to make, but I think it was clear that upon confirmation to a chair, and if there was not a continuing criminal probe—which J. Powell certainly doesn't think was justified, and many share that view—that he would, you know, leave the board at the end of his term. So let's see how this plays out. But the main thing I think that's important is that Treasury Secretary Bessent has a good Fed chair that has the confidence of the President and who can continue to lead economic policy. And for those of us in Congress—Chairman Scott in the Senate, our Financial Services Committee of the House—we want the ability to conduct oversight of the Fed, and it's very hard to do that when you don't have a permanent Senate-confirmed chair. Well, but on that oversight idea, do you think it's right that the Fed IG is the one taking this over rather than having the banking committee or the Financial Services Committee in that oversight capacity taking this on? Right, such a good point of view. The Fed IG is a very competent person who I think fully has the capacity to do it and has the staff and the resources. I know, speaking for myself, I was very happy to help lead an independent view by the Congress, and I can't speak for Chairman Scott, but I think he would share that view that we're happy to conduct oversight as needed on the expense and the construction parameters around the Fed's renovations. What's your view about what's happening with the Eccles Building? We've all had work done on our homes that went way over cost and beyond timelines; this is frequently the essence of doing really major renovations, which are happening in that case to a very old building. Do you think that there's been irresponsible behavior or this is the cost of doing business? "I wouldn't be able to answer that question," Hill replies. The President's a builder; he's got strong feelings about that. He went over and took a look himself. And the issue is that when you are doing construction in the District of Columbia, I can only imagine how expensive that is on permitting and required union labor and historic renovations and archaeological remains and all those costs. And then the inflation that was unleashed by the Fed's own policy in 2020 and 2021, along with the rapid spending by the previous administration. So everyone, you're right, is facing that, whether it's in your home or in a commercial context. But the President raised the issue that this seems disproportionately large compared to other renovation plans that he's seen personally, here in Washington, D.C. in his own experience. So it deserves explanation. The question is: did J. Powell do something criminal about it? I just reject that point of view. Well, aside from the building itself, obviously this now leaves major monetary policy trajectory questions if Kevin Warsh is going to be taking the helm, given what we have heard from him publicly. A person who used to be quite hawkish now seems decidedly less so. He also suggested in his confirmation hearing that he would like to do away with the amount of forward guidance given by the Federal Reserve. And I wonder if you think that would be a mistake—to, for example, not put out the summary of economic projections. Well, the Fed has fine-tuned how it conducts public communication between post-meeting press conferences, forward guidance, the dot plots, and the approved speeches that individual governors make. It's up to the Board of Governors about that; there's no statute that says that they have to. But do you think we hear from them too much? Does it tie their hands? Because that's what Kevin Warsh argues. Right, you know, I think that's his opinion. He's the chairman; let's see what consensus he has. I don't know that how much I hear from the Fed determines what my opinion is about how the Fed's doing. You know, they have 700 PhD economists; sometimes I think the market tells them where they ought to be a lot better than some of their economic forecasts, which haven't been so great. But let's see how the new chair, once he's confirmed, works with his colleagues to outline what the public position will be. Yeah, I don't know. Sometimes it's good to ask a community banker about his insights on these things. But the idea of a new regime, a new framework, a new approach to making interest rate decisions—does it make you nervous or optimistic? Is that needed? No, I think it doesn't make me nervous at all. In fact, I asked Frank Lucas of Oklahoma to chair a special task force in this Congress to look at the past decisions by the Fed's monetary policy and the Treasury market structure. These are critical issues that we face right now. Frank has conducted oversight on the size of the balance sheet, the focus on the dual mandate, and whether it should be mostly focused on price stability. How does it interact with the banking community on liquidity? So we've actually been investigating these questions ourselves—how the district banks participate. So the fact that Kevin comes in as a former governor, having worked through the financial crisis and having his viewpoints on how the Fed should operate, I think that's healthy. I think we need a rejuvenation in our institutions from time to time, and that's why we have the advising and consent confirmation process in the Senate and why these Fed chairs have a five-year term. Not everybody can serve four presidents like Alan Greenspan can. Of course, we're having this conversation, and this potential turnover at the top of the Fed, at a time where the global economy—at least a portion of it—is being held in a chokehold via the Strait of Hormuz. The US is maintaining a naval blockade and Iran is still making it unsafe for commercial vessels to transit. So long as that status quo remains, the President said yesterday he is in no rush to solve this; he has all the time in the world. Does the economy actually have all the time in the world for this situation? I think we're all clear that the longer the oil fuel markets are disrupted by the transit in the Gulf, we have price concerns and volatility concerns. I'm watching that forward market in oil prices to see if they fully reflect that concern, and I do think that if the war continues, it could be underestimated in the market. So the question will be—I think the President though is right to do something that we've wanted to do for four decades, which is end the malfeasance, murder, and mayhem at the hands of the Iranians. Stop their ability to have a nuclear weapon and stop their ability to harass their neighbors all the way to Paris, France, through threats of a ballistic missile and their export of terror through their proxies. These are important goals. They're shared goals, whether people are out in the press saying their shared goals are not; they have been long-term shared goals. And so I do commend the President for taking that action. But we do have economic risks associated with how long it's maintained. There's no doubt about that. Mr. Chairman, we really appreciate your being with us in our Washington Bureau today. Andreessen Horowitz is backing UDC as a startup building autonomous underwater vehicles, pointing to a new frontier in ocean and defense tech. Aaron Price Wright is a general partner at Andreessen Horowitz. He just did that round in the last week or so and joins us on the show. I find this so interesting. I think over the course of the war in Iran, we focus a lot on drone technology and other missile technology, but with the Strait of Hormuz in particular, now that domain is becoming increasingly important. I think just explain your conviction and thesis with UDC as a case study. "Yeah, first thank you so much for having me. It's really wonderful to be here," Wright says. The ocean is largely unexplored and unconquered territory, and it's kind of remarkable how little innovation there has been in maritime over the last half century, despite how much it's grown in strategic importance for the United States and globally. I mean, if you think about the beginning of the conflict in Ukraine, the first thing that Russia did was cut Ukraine's undersea fiber so that they didn't have any internet access. You just mentioned we're seeing this again in Iran with the strait and how much global tension and conflict that's caused. And expect something similar if we were ever to be in conflict in the Pacific theater with the South China Sea. So, you know, really this was a market and an area very much hungry for innovation. If you look at our submarine capabilities, they haven't really advanced in half a century. It costs billions of dollars and these are people who are putting their lives at risk. Many of the things that we need to worry about in the ocean today actually don't need manned crews to be able to accomplish really important missions. Whether it's on the commercial side from inspecting oil and gas, or deep sea mining, or offshore wind, or other assets through to the defense space—a lot of those use cases don't need manned crews. I just want to point out Aaron, if you bear with me, that right now the story in public markets is stocks are higher, technology stocks in particular, on hope and optimism. Over this weekend, there will be peace talks between the United States and Iran. And we're here principally to talk about private markets, but I think there's been a bit of a disconnect in understanding something that's happening live in real time—a conflict like the one in Iran—and how that's given momentum to the industries and areas that you want to invest in. Has it got people moving? "Yes, it's a really, really good question, and it has," Wright says. I think it's been a real forcing function. It's put a magnifying glass up to our defense space and how ill-prepared we would be for a major conflict, and it's helped speed up a lot of the procurement processes and conversations with the Department of War. We're seeing the Department of War come to our early-stage companies and say, "How fast can you deliver a hundred? How fast can you deliver a thousand? How fast can you deliver ten thousand?" They are really starting to change their thinking around what the requirements are, what matters, and how do we make sure that we have the production scale capacity to be able to fight in a future conflict. So I'm very hopeful for peace in the Middle East, and also this has been a real wake-up call for our defense department—for what we really have to do and how we have to support the defense industrial base in order to be prepared for a future conflict. You know, even in the domain of the ocean, Anduril is an interesting case study as well, right? They have focused a lot, not just on the technology and autonomous vehicles in the water, but where they're placed around the world. But one data set—our Bloomberg tech producer Zoe Thomas always tracks the declassified spends—shows that where that dollar goes, it still goes to Lockheed Martin, even in the aquatic context. Is that the same story—that even if Anduril and UDC do move fast, the legacy primes are getting all of the government spending? Well, let's take Ulysses as the example. They didn't start the company as a defense prime. They started the company where the first use cases were seagrass planting, seafloor restoration, energy inspection, and port inspection. Essentially, they were working on use cases that had very strong cost curves where their customers couldn't pay more than 40, 50, or $60,000 for a unit to make the economics work. So they had to really focus on high-quality engineering to build a very low-cost and highly manufacturable, scalable product for their commercial customers. And you know, they have lots of operational experience in the field fielding these units for real use cases in the ocean. We're talking 40, 50, or $60,000 a pop for their UUVs. The Defense Department comes and sees that and they're like, "You've got to be kidding me. We're paying for the exact same thing, and we're paying two, three, four, or five million dollars a pop. Are you telling me I can buy this for $40,000?" So they have work to do to catch up in terms of proving out their tech in real missions. But when you're talking about two or three orders of magnitude cost difference for virtually the same thing, I think it's just a matter of time before we see these defense tech startups start to catch up to the primes. Aaron Price Wright, of Andreessen Horowitz, great to have you on the program. Thank you. Now coming up, tech earnings are underway and next week is a big one. We're going to discuss what to expect.
