Former Binance CEO Speaks: From Crypto's Richest Man to Prison

The Free Press(450.0K) ·65:57 ·601 views · 2026-04-24
Changpeng Zhao (Former CEO, Binance)
Former Binance CEO Changpeng Zhao (CZ) details his transition from the head of the world's largest crypto exchange to a federal inmate, addressing his $4.3 billion DOJ settlement, his controversial presidential pardon, and his new focus on global education philanthropy.
TL;DR Former Binance CEO Changpeng Zhao (CZ) details his transition from the head of the world's largest crypto exchange to a federal inmate, addressing his $4.3 billion DOJ settlement, his controversial presidential pardon, and his new focus on global education philanthropy. ...

TL;DR

Former Binance CEO Changpeng Zhao (CZ) details his transition from the head of the world's largest crypto exchange to a federal inmate, addressing his $4.3 billion DOJ settlement, his controversial presidential pardon, and his new focus on global education philanthropy.

Legal Accountability and the US Prison Experience

  • DOJ Guilty Plea: CZ and Binance pled guilty to violating the U.S. Bank Secrecy Act (BSA) due to an ineffective anti-money laundering program during the company’s early growth phase (2017–2019).
      • Financial Penalties: Binance paid a $4.3 billion fine, while CZ personally paid $50 million and stepped down as CEO.
      • Addressing Terrorism Allegations: CZ maintains that while compliance was "weak" and "inexperienced," the court found no evidence that Binance actively or intentionally facilitated terrorist financing.
  • Incarceration Details: Because CZ is not a U.S. citizen, he was ineligible for a minimum-security "camp" and served four months in a higher-security correctional facility in Long Beach, California.
      • Prison Etiquette: He hired "prison consultants" (ex-wardens/guards) to learn survival strategies, such as never cutting in line and managing group dynamics to avoid extortion.
      • Richest Inmate: Despite his net worth, CZ lived among the general population, teaching fellow inmates and some guards about cryptocurrency.

The Trump Pardon and Political Allegations

  • The Pardon Context: CZ was pardoned by President Trump in April 2025. He attributes this to Trump’s broader pro-crypto stance, noting similar pardons for Ross Ulbricht (Silk Road) and Arthur Hayes (BitMEX).
  • Denying Quid Pro Quo: CZ firmly rejected allegations that his pardon was linked to Binance’s dealings with the Trump family's crypto venture, World Liberty Financial.
      • USD1 Stablecoin: While Binance listed the Trump-affiliated USD1 stablecoin, CZ claims the decision was driven by an Emirati investment firm (MGX) choosing it as a transaction medium, rather than a direct deal with the Trumps.
      • Personal Relationship: CZ states he has never met, messaged, or spoken with Donald Trump, characterizing the pardon process as a "black box."
  • Conflict of Interest: CZ argues that Trump’s crypto ventures are no more a conflict of interest than a president holding stocks, as broad pro-crypto policies benefit the entire global industry rather than just one family.

The Future of the Crypto Industry and Regulation

  • Global Adoption: CZ identifies the U.S. as a potential global leader in crypto if the current pro-innovation pace continues, though he highlights the UAE (specifically Abu Dhabi) as a strong competitor.
      • State Reserves: He cites Bhutan (mining with hydropower) and El Salvador (holding Bitcoin) as successful examples of nation-state adoption despite market volatility.
  • The "Meme Coin" Phenomenon: CZ views the rise of meme coins as a byproduct of aggressive SEC enforcement against "utility" projects; he believes they should remain a small, speculative part of the ecosystem rather than its focus.
  • SBF and FTX: CZ avoids judging the fairness of Sam Bankman-Fried’s 25-year sentence but challenges the narrative that "no one lost money," noting that creditors are being paid back at 2022 prices rather than the significantly higher current market values.

Binance Strategy and Philanthropy

  • IPO Potential: While CZ no longer manages Binance's daily operations, he notes that an IPO remains a viable future option for the company to gain credibility and traditional capital, though no immediate plans are in place.
  • Giggle Academy: CZ’s primary focus is now a free, gamified basic education platform for children (ages 2-6 currently), which currently serves 240,000 students.
  • Wealth Distribution: CZ intends to give away 90% to 99% of his wealth, though he admits that "giving money away well" is a difficult task requiring significant time and management.
  • Satoshi Nakamoto: When asked about the identity of Bitcoin's creator, CZ speculates it was most likely the late Hal Finney, citing the fact that the original coins have never moved.
"There's no police force in the world that's 100% effective... we agree that our earlier compliance program was weak... we were inexperienced, we thought we were good. Later on, we said, well, we could have done better." — Changpeng Zhao (CZ)
"The president, the most powerful person on the planet, he's going to make a lot of impactful decisions... why is it only in crypto that his family... having some holdings in crypto companies [is seen as] a conflict of interest?" — Changpeng Zhao (CZ)
"I had a very happy childhood... [with] dirt floors, no running water, no electricity. When you don't have something, you don't know what you're missing... I'm still a little child inside." — Changpeng Zhao (CZ)
Some of you may already know the name, Changpeng Zhao, or CZ, as he likes to go by. He’s almost universally known in the tech and finance worlds as crypto’s richest man. Forbes calculated his net worth as greater than $110 billion, which puts him ahead of Bill Gates, though CZ denies the Forbes estimate. He amassed this wealth by founding Binance, the world’s largest cryptocurrency exchange, transforming it from a startup in 2017 into one of the most powerful companies in finance. Then things took a turn. In November 2023, CZ pled guilty to violating the US Bank Secrecy Act by failing to maintain an effective anti-money laundering program. He stepped down as CEO and personally paid a $50 million fine, while Binance paid a $4.3 billion fine. When I asked him about this charge, he acknowledged that Binance’s earlier compliance program was weak, and that Binance could have done better. In all, the case led to a four-month sentence at a correctional facility in Long Beach, California, where he taught fellow inmates and even some guards about cryptocurrency. He also spent his time writing his newly released memoir, Freedom of Money, a memoir of protecting users, resilience, and the founding of Binance. In our conversation, I ask him about this DOJ case against him and Binance, and the controversy surrounding his pardon, which was issued by President Trump about a year later, and the alleged business dealings with the Trump family. I ask what he thinks about President Trump profiting from crypto ventures, whether he thinks it's ethical for an incoming president to launch a meme coin, and if he thinks Sam Bankman-Fried, whose firm Binance once invested in, was treated fairly in US courts. I also get into why terrorist organizations and criminal networks are drawn to cryptocurrency, and whether there's really an effective way to prevent that kind of illegal activity. We ended by discussing the possibility of a Binance IPO, who he believes Satoshi Nakamoto really is, and why CZ describes himself as pretty happy growing up in rural China, in a home with a dirt floor and no running water. As I was reading the book, he seems just as happy in that phase of his life as he is today. I learned so much from this conversation, and I think you will too. Stay with us. CZ, thank you so much for being here. Well, thank you for having me. You were born in rural China, and now you have more money than Bill Gates. And what’s so interesting about all of this is that a lot of people don’t even know who you are—people who aren’t cryptoheads, for example. And so I want to start at the very beginning. Tell us a little bit more about the family that you were born into. Yeah. Well, first of all, I don't think I'm richer than Bill Gates. I think the calculations by Forbes is wrong, but anyway. Yeah, I grew up—I was born in a small village and my mom is a math teacher, a high school teacher, and then my dad is an electrician. I grew up in a, you know, village environment in China before I was 10. We had like dirt floors, no running water, no electricity. I did some of my elementary homework under an oil lamp. And then I moved to a slightly bigger city called Hefei. And then at 12 I went to Vancouver, and then I did college in Montreal. And then I worked in Tokyo, then New York, then Shanghai. And then in 2013 I found Bitcoin, and then four years later we launched Binance. And we got lucky; Binance grew pretty big. Yeah. To recap what you just said, you moved from rural China to Shanghai to Vancouver. One thing you didn’t mention that I learned in the book is that you worked at a McDonald’s, you worked at a gas station. You end up on the Tokyo Stock Exchange, Wall Street. That’s already an evolution in and of itself. But then in 2013, your life really changed when you discovered crypto. What was it about crypto that made you want to basically turn your life upside down to pursue this budding industry? Yeah, for me crypto was like the technology that increases the freedom of money, right? So if internet increased the freedom of information—and I, you know, when internet happened, I was just in college, out of college around 2000. So I was too young to take advantage of it. And then I thought, you know, this, crypto, blockchain is the same order of magnitude of technology shift as internet. So I was like, "Okay, I'm not going to miss this, I want to get in early." So I sold my house, sold my little apartment, quit my job, and then went all in on crypto. I just understood it as a fundamental technology that will change everything, similar to AI today. At the time—like, this is what, in 2013, 13 years ago—I thought the next technology that comes along maybe 15 years later. Now it's AI, right? So I think these three—internet, blockchain and AI—are the three fundamental technologies in my adult life so far. So at 40, you founded Binance. It made you a billionaire. It became the world’s largest crypto exchange. Big picture, tell us what it was about you and your team that made Binance so successful. Yeah. So I think basically, fundamentally, what we did differently is we protected users really, really well. I think before us, there were many, many crypto exchanges. Even afterwards, there were many, many crypto exchanges. But because we're in the crypto industry, we deal with money, there's a lot of quick money. So many people are kind of attracted by the short-term returns and they try to keep money for themselves, and they sometimes don't protect users as well as we do. So we do many, many things in benefit of our users, sometimes at the short-term cost to us. But we earn enough fees so that we are sufficiently funded. And then we just grow really big because we actually make less money—because we make less money per transaction and we protect our users in a lot of instances where we have to put up the money. But we’ve grown really big in terms of user base. And when the user base grown really big, we benefited from the scale. So I think many people just didn't have this equation in their head. They always trying to get a little bit more from each transaction. We're like, "No, let's get a little bit less from each transaction and then have scale." So I think that was one of the biggest things. There's many other things where we got a bit lucky on. We started in the sort of ICO boom year in 2017. And then we had a strong team that worked really, really hard and they executed well. And ICO for listeners, it’s like an IPO but for a coin. I just say that because a lot of people that listen are not super familiar with crypto terms and things. But it is true, like in reading your book, I was fascinated by some of the anecdotes of like, you know, typically if people send crypto to the wrong address, it’s gone for them. And I thought it was really interesting to read about how your team took bespoke care to try to help people even though it was so expensive and did nothing for your business. So some of those anecdotes I thought were really interesting. I want to get into some of the most interesting parts of the book. And I think one element of the book that will, you know, have a lightbulb moment for people who don’t follow crypto so closely are some of your anecdotes in and around Sam Bankman-Fried. Of course, Sam Bankman-Fried founded FTX, which was another exchange, an exchange that competed with Binance. And you met Sam Bankman-Fried in 2019. In January 2022, my understanding is that FTX was valued around 32 billion. By November of that same year, it was on a path to insolvency. And he calls you asking for a couple billion dollars, really casual. He suggests selling you FTX. Ultimately, you don’t buy it. The exchange crashes. I think people will remember how shocking that was. And he’s charged with fraud and now he serves 25 years in jail. And I’m wondering, you know, what’s interesting to me in all of this is that 25 years. Do you think the law was fair on Sam Bankman-Fried or do you think it went too far? I, this is a very hard one for me to comment. Based on public information that I know, he took somewhere between $4 to $8 billion of user money and made investments, used that to buy tokens himself. And he did not disclose that to the public. I only learned that much, much later, right? So how you classify that, and then he lost most of the money and then, you know, there was a liquidity crunch—people could not withdraw. So I yeah, so I I'm not in a position to comment on whether the sentencing was fair or not, etc. Yeah, so it is what it is. It is what it is. I think some people wonder about it because it’s like, my understanding is no one lost money and most of the investors were not American, and so it sort of just questions like, you know, was it overreach? So yeah, well, on this point, I think when the statement that no one lost money is actually not necessarily true. I think based on US dollar terms, using crypto prices at the 2022 price, no one lost money. But back then, Bitcoin was what, 16,000, 20,000. If people held those crypto—if my understanding is they're compensating people based on 2022 crypto prices. If they're compensating people based on today's crypto prices, $70, $75,000, or at the high point, it was up to $125,000 per Bitcoin. My understanding is that's not the case what they're doing, right? So if you had crypto on FTX, you have one Bitcoin, now they gave you the Bitcoin at the 2022 price instead of today's price, which is 75k. So to say that nobody lost money, depending how how you calculated it, yeah. People lost the potential to make because of the misappropriation, is sort of what you’re saying. Yeah, yeah. So if people held their own Bitcoin until today, they would have they would have had a lot more money, yeah, than what they're getting from FTX. And also some international users—I don't know the details of this, but I read on Twitter that many of the Chinese users, for example, were not compensated because they said, I don't know, so this, I think there's still quite a lot of people who lost money, yeah. That’s a good point. As most people know, you had some legal issues of your own in November 2023. Binance pled guilty to violating anti-money laundering laws, which allegedly allowed money to flow to terrorists and cyber criminals. And, you know, it’s been almost three years since that chapter. You know, what do you think Binance looking back actually did wrong or could have done differently three years later? So both Binance and myself, we pled guilty to a federal charge, the Banking Secrecy Act. For Binance, I think there were more discussions about, you know, compliance programs, etc. And even though I was the CEO of Binance at the time and I handled some of the case negotiations. But my case was Binance violated the BSA law in the US, which basically says if you run a financial platform, you need to register in the US and you need to have a proper KYC program. So Binance.com, the global entity, did not register in the US. There's a separate arm called Binance.US that registered in the US. But Binance the global entity, when it first started before I think between 2017 and early 2019ish, they did have US users. So this is a fact, right? So for this part, they said, "No, you're violating the BSA law." And then at the time, the compliance program was not strong enough. But there were a lot of talks trying to say, "Look, Binance facilitated terrorist financing." That never happened, right? So Binance never actively, intentionally facilitated terrorist financing. Having a weak compliance program—you got to understand that no compliance program in the world is 100%. Even like the top banks, they will have some transactions that would be viewed as illicit or associated with bad actors, etc., right? So there's no police force in the world that's 100% effective. And then that's the part that was being debated and argued, etc. But there was no active—well, there were and there are no active, intentional facilitation of terrorist financing, etc. I think that's just a lot of the media trying to misquote some of the... there were allegations of it, but they were they were never proved. In fact, the court in my case—the court completely rejected the government's claim saying that there was no evidence for that charge. Well, I don’t think you, I don’t think anyone at Binance would want, you know, terrorists to get money, but should there have been more oversight, I think is like maybe some of the question that people have. And I wonder if you could go back in time if you would have done things differently in terms of building out or thinking about, you know, the oversight structure that was at Binance. Sure, sure. No, I think look, we we agree that our earlier compliance program was weak, right? We started as a technology company in Shanghai. And then two months later, six weeks later, we had to move out of Shanghai and then we were moving around different places. And we started as a technology platform, right? So and the platform grew really, really quickly. So our initial compliance program, which we did have from the early day—from the very, very early days we had KYC programs; we used Sumsub, we used Jumio to do KYC. But how strong they are, we were not perfect. So but at the same time though, people need to understand that there's there's no standard to say, "Look, you're compliant"—it's not a binary thing. There's no standard saying your compliance program is good enough, right? It's all subjective. So we at the time we were inexperienced; we thought we were good. Later on we said, "Well, we could we could have done better." So that's kind of the situation, yeah. You know, this sort of is tied up in this other conversation around some of the taboos around crypto. And I think some people ask like, is there really a good way to prevent this type of illegal activity? You know, because some might say, you know, one of the points of cryptocurrency is that it’s designed to facilitate decentralized transactions. The traditional media like to portray crypto as, you know, this place where all these illicit transactions happen. But this both percentage-wise... percentage-wise like there's a hundred times less transactions in crypto, less illicit transactions in crypto by volume. And then there's like probably 10,000 times less amount-wise. So there's a very common misconception. And I can tell you why. Crypto is very easy to trace, and people are very good at tracing it. Crypto is one single public blockchain, Bitcoin, right? So it's very—and it's permanent. It's always there. So anybody can look at it. People say it's pseudo-anonymous, but if you tie a KYC information from large exchanges, you can pretty much know every address. So people are very good at labeling labeling those addresses now. So it's actually extremely hard to do illicit transactions in crypto without people knowing. My follow-up question: you know, someone might say then why would the IRGC want payments from boats passing through the Strait of Hormuz to be in crypto? Or why would people choose crypto to send money to Hamas? Like if it’s, you know, like like why is crypto their choice of their vehicle to move money? So crypto is very easy to trace, but if you run your own crypto wallet, it's hard to block. So you can see it, but you may not be able to block it, right? So the IRGC, the Iran situation is like there's an open war. They said, "Look, we're just going to receive Bitcoin." I can tell you that the US government is tracking every single transaction to IRGC. So whereas if they do through banks, they actually they actually can't see it, right? So they have to subpoena to get information, there's a lot of different banks in the world. It's actually hard to get that information. Whereas on the blockchain, it's very very easy to trace. But if you use raw Bitcoin, if you have your own self-custody wallet and somebody else is sending from their own self-custody wallet, the network will not reject a transaction and there's no way for other people to block it. So you have sovereignty in that sense, but it's very—it's very traceable. So it’s very traceable, but who has the power to block it? Actually, for Bitcoin, if you don't use a centralized exchange, then no one. On the centralized exchange, then each operator—each platform—does have the power to block it. But if you just use native Bitcoin, then no one can block it. This opens up a really interesting philosophical question that I was thinking about, which is: you know, on one hand people say it enables these black market transactions, everything that we’ve been talking about, and people don’t like that. At the same time, there’s sort of this interesting contradiction, which is that, you know, one core philosophy, I think—you’ll correct me if I’m wrong—of crypto is that no government or intermediary should get to decide who is allowed to participate. You know, and I think maybe some people have tension with this idea of someone coming in to block, even if, you know, that payment, you know, would go to someone like the IRGC or like a terrorist organization regardless of their sanctioning. And I wonder how you think about that sort of contradiction between the ethos of, you know, maybe some of the early founders of crypto and some of like the... what centralized exchanges might be incentivized to do. There is a balance here. At certain times, you—we want law enforcement to be able to freeze funds to chase bad actors, etc. In the other extreme, I'm not sure if you want government... there are cases where bad actors, even in the law enforcement space or governments, are freezing people's funds unilaterally without good reason. And also and also if you think about it, right? You work very hard to make your own money, but the government can make you poor arbitrarily by printing more money, right? So let's say there's $100 in circulation. If the government prints another $100 and puts that in circulation, you just become poor by 50% without doing anything, right? So you work really hard for your money, you pay your taxes, and suddenly somebody makes you 50% poor. In some developed countries, this quantitative easing, inflation, printing some money is is sometimes useful, it's not always bad. But doing that excessively is bad, right? So there's many different governments in the world. Some governments do it very, very aggressively, some some governments don't. So whether that's good or not, everybody can decide for themselves. But with crypto, with Bitcoin, no, there's it's a fixed supply. So number one, no one's going to print more of that at will, right? So no one has the power. If you want to print more of that, you can go buy mining machines and there are less and less of that coming out. There's only like, I don't know, there's only less than a million Bitcoins left out of 21 million total supply, right? So that's maximum 5% more. And we know the speed of that coming out. So that protects you against dilution or inflation, right? So do you want that or not? Then it's for people to decide. The ability to transact with anyone in the world that you wish to—again, the technology gives you control as the owner of your Bitcoin. And whether you think that government should have the ability to freeze your money at will, it's up to you, right? So different countries have different laws. I think we as a as platform operators... the platform operators should follow laws in every country where they operate. As an individual, I think if you live in a country that has a law that prevents you from owning your own assets with that level of control—if your country blocks you, says it's illegal to own your Bitcoin—then you shouldn't touch Bitcoin. If you live in a country that says holding Bitcoin is perfectly legal, then you can hold your Bitcoin and you can use Bitcoin's properties. So that's kind of the way I view it—is there's a philosophical component to it, there's a legal component to what you can do in your own country, and there's what you choose to do within those frameworks, yeah. But I guess like, do you think blocking certain entities based on politics, you know, cuts against some of like the very OG crypto ethos? The hardcore crypto guys say, "Look, you should have control over your wealth and you should have control over your money and who you transact with." At the government political level, like what you talked about is basically sanctions, right? So sanctions are implemented in a lot of different places; says, "Look, you can't do business with this country, you can't do business with this individual," etc. Those those have been there for a while. Whether they are good or bad is really up to each individual. Some countries have very strong, like extensive sanction programs—namely the US and Europe—and those are very strong laws that everyone everyone needs to follow. There are countries without those sort of sanction rules, and then, you know, people are more free to transact. So yeah, so I I wouldn't be able to comment on which laws make sense, which don't. It's just how the world works. Basically, look, the strong country says, "Look, because we're so strong, if you want to be if you want to do business with us, if you want to be friends with us, you can't be friends with others." Whether that's good or bad, it's up to everyone's own judgment. Well, ultimately, you know, you had to pay 50 million, Binance had to pay 4.3 billion—it’s a lot of money. You stepped down as CEO, you served four months in prison. Interestingly, you were the wealthiest inmate in US history. And I heard you had prison consultants, which I thought were only in movies, but apparently not. What were the most valuable lessons that you learned from them? So in the US alone, there's about 2 million people in prison. And it's growing every year. It's like growing at double-digit percentages, right? So it's an industry that grows, and the people who service those industries want the industry to grow, want the inmate numbers to grow. Because like, you know, think about it—if you're a supplier of food for prison, there's no customer feedback, right? There's no customer satisfaction you got to worry about. No, it's just food for prisoners. It's a very good business—very steady, fairly large as well. So there are people who are incentivized to make the prison population bigger at the expense of the government's spendings, right? So that's one thing. The prison consultant is a big industry, too. Like what did they teach you? So they they taught me some useful things. Like no, so I had a four-month sentence, right? So they—one thing is like, look, you can calculate the number of days you got to spend there. If you calculate your last day to be Sunday, then you get released on Friday, which means like—now—you stay two days less in prison. So that thing I didn't know about. They also... there's a lot of a lot of rules that are up for interpretation, right? So which kind of prison do I go to? Do I go to a medium-security prison or a minimum-security prison? Minimum-security prison is what they call a camp, right? How do I apply for it? What kind of rules qualify me or not disqualify me for that? Because I'm a—not—a US citizen. Can I get an exception? Like, the exceptions are not unusual. They are actually quite common, but I—I—didn't get one. But there was a lot of attempts in getting one. You didn’t go to, even though you were white collar, you didn’t go to a minimum security because you’re not a US citizen, so you actually you had to go to a rung up, which is arguably more dangerous and so forth. Yeah. No, I appreciate you pointing that out. So and then like, "How do I get to..." so most people with less than 6 to 18 months on their prison sentence get transferred to home confinement, right? So how do I... like, with a four-month sentence, when do I apply, how do I get it? So there's a lot of discussions about that. And also like even little things in prison, like how do you act in prison? What do you expect, right? What's the prison etiquette? Like, now, never cut in line. Don't piss people off. Do you join a group quickly or do you stay alone? So if you stay alone, then you're a loner—many people may take advantage of you. If you join a group, then you may have to satisfy sort of the group requirements which may—I don't know—how do you avoid extortion? So there's a lot of topics that’ve been discussed. What's interesting is the US population's so large, no one knows everything. The prison consultants don't even know everything. The prison consultants are typically ex-wardens or ex-guards that worked in a prison. But then they were never inmates in a prison. And each prison is different. Out of 2 million people in prison, each prison houses about 2 to 3,000 people. They are like mini cities on their own. But there's still thousands of prisons in the US. And every state has different laws. It's a very, very complex thing. Most people think, "Oh, you go to prison, you just go to prison and then, you know, good behavior you get a third time less and you get out." But there were so many details in that. So most people don't even know about that, yeah. People will have to read the book because there’s a lot of very interesting details here. Like one that I remember is, you know, they obviously self-segregate prisons because they think that’ll be less violent. So they put you with Asians, but like there weren’t enough Asians, so they put the indigenous with you guys. Like there’s just a lot of like very interesting detail here. And people will have to buy and read the book for all of that. Thank you for that. You know, obviously you do these four months, you’re released in September 2024. About a year later, you’re pardoned. And you know, one of the things here is that when you were pardoned, Wall Street Journal and a bunch of others claimed that it was because Binance had done a series of transactions to help the Trump family crypto company World Liberty Financial and their stablecoin USD1. And the allegation was that, you know, these sort of friendly dealings and this relationship ultimately led to your pardon. And so I want to just give you a chance to respond to those allegations and say why you think you were pardoned. So first of all, the media started covering my pardon even before I applied for a pardon. So Wall Street Journal and Bloomberg wrote that I'm trying to get a pardon in January, February, and March of 2025. I only applied for a pardon in April 2025. So it was like, actually, because of those articles saying that I am probably getting a pardon, I was like, "Well, maybe I should apply, right?" So I got a lawyer and, no, we filed a petition in April 2025. And also to give the listener the context: three days into Trump's administration, he declares he's pro-crypto, right? Three days into his administration, he pardons Ross Ulbricht. He is the Silk Road founder who was sentenced for double life back to back, right? For operating Silk Road. Silk Road sold like, I don't know, drugs, other crazy stuff. Binance did not, right? So and then in March 2025, President Trump also pardoned Arthur Hayes, who was the owner of BitMEX, which is a crypto futures exchange. And Arthur pleaded to a single violation of the Banking Secrecy Act. He never went to jail. He had done home confinement. And then he was pardoned. So I was like, "Okay, so there's two cases where the president is looking at crypto cases." So my case, I think compared to Arthur—like, if those guys get pardoned then I should be very eligible for it. So that's one thing. And then to address the transaction side. There's been no transactions between me and President Trump or his or his families, right? So that just doesn't that just doesn't exist. So and then also on the stablecoin side. USD1 is the second largest genius compliant stablecoin. Binance is the largest exchange. So Binance basically has to list it, right? So if Binance doesn't list it, then Binance will lose market share. So it's actually in Binance's benefit to list it. And also I actually inquired about the terms a little bit. Binance—for USD1 stablecoin listing on Binance—Binance gets much more favorable terms than any other stablecoin. So Binance benefits from this transaction more than the Trump family by the industry standard. So there's a lot of misconception out there. There's no transactions. Is a Recession Coming? What 100+ Companies Are Telling Us | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(171.0K) ·31:43 ·64.4K views · 2026-04-24
Steve Eisman (Host)
> "The global financial system functions on Treasuries.
TL;DR: Q1 2026 earnings results from over 100 companies suggest that a recession is not imminent; however, high interest rates and geopolitical tensions are creating a "precarious" environment for software and homebuilders while disrupting the long-standing FICO monopoly. ...

TL;DR: Q1 2026 earnings results from over 100 companies suggest that a recession is not imminent; however, high interest rates and geopolitical tensions are creating a "precarious" environment for software and homebuilders while disrupting the long-standing FICO monopoly.

Macro Outlook and Private Credit

  • Recession Status: Broad earnings data indicates a resilient economy. While the war in Iran has increased oil prices—leading United and American Airlines to cut 2026 guidance—bank credit quality remains benign.
  • Blackstone (BCRED): The flagship $81 billion private credit fund saw performance flatten in March.
      • Write-downs: Portfolio value was dragged down by Medallia and Affordable Care, marked at 60.3 and 69.8 cents on the dollar, respectively.
      • Credit Quality: Non-accruals increased to 1.4% of fair value.
  • US Debt Crisis: Despite warnings from former Treasury Secretary Hank Paulson regarding the 125% debt-to-GDP ratio, Eisman remains unconcerned. He argues that there is currently no liquid alternative to the U.S. Treasury for the global repo market.

The End of the FICO Monopoly

  • Regulatory Shift: The FHFA announced that Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac will now accept VantageScore as an alternative to FICO for mortgage approvals.
  • Market Impact: This concludes FICO’s decades-long monopoly on GSE mortgage world scores. The stock has dropped over 40% this year; Eisman maintains a short position.

Industrial Growth and the "AI Tail"

  • GE Aerospace: Reported 25% EPS growth and a massive 87% increase in total orders ($23 billion), signaling that airlines are not yet pulling back on expansion despite geopolitical opaque-ness.
  • GE Vernova (GEV): A primary beneficiary of the AI data center boom.
      • Demand: AI centers are driving a 3% annual growth in US electrical production. GEV is one of only three global manufacturers of the gas turbines required to meet this demand.
      • Growth: EPS rose 85% year-over-year; orders reached $18.3 billion (up 71%).
  • Tesla: Eisman characterizes the stock as a "dream" play. While auto fundamentals are weak and the 2026 PE is over 200x, bulls are focused entirely on Elon Musk’s $25 billion annual CapEx commitment to autonomous vehicles and robotics.

Sector Turnarounds: Healthcare and Software

  • Managed Care: The industry shows signs of stabilization.
      • United Healthcare (UNH): Reported a beat ($7.23 EPS vs. $6.57 expected) driven by better Medical Loss Ratios (MLR), though issues remain in the Optum division due to new Medicare coding penalties.
      • Molina: Faces headwinds as the government prunes Medicaid rolls, leaving behind a sicker, more expensive cohort.
  • Software Risks: Eisman warns against "bottom fishing" in software.
      • ServiceNow: Despite 22% revenue growth, the stock fell 13% on news of delayed deal closings in the Middle East.
      • The AI Threat: Eisman believes AI will slow "seat growth" and erode the pricing power software companies have enjoyed for decades.

Housing and Interest Rates

  • Homebuilders: The 10-year Treasury yield hitting 4.4% has severely hampered the spring selling season.
      • D.R. Horton (DHI): EPS fell 13% year-over-year, but the stock rallied because results were "not as bad" as feared.
      • Meritage Homes: Trading below its tangible book value of $75. The company is pivoting from land acquisition to share repurchases (3% of shares retired this quarter).

If and when there is a large liquid alternative to Treasuries, I will become more worried about the deficit." — Steve Eisman

Given that I've already mentioned private credit, let's start with the earnings of Blackstone which reported Thursday morning. The stock moved lower on Thursday as investors weighed a beat on one key metric against falling returns within its massive private credit and real estate businesses. The metric represents the cash flow available to be paid as dividends to shareholders. Now before we move on to other earnings, I want to flag news on Fair Isaac, aka FICO. FICO is the backbone of the entire consumer lending industry. When a consumer applies for any kind of consumer loan, the lender, whoever that is, pulls a FICO score from Fair Isaac. That score is the largest determinant of whether the consumer gets the loan. FICO has had a monopoly on this process for decades. No longer. The FHFA, which is the regulator of Fannie and Freddie, announced this week that the GSEs will start accepting VantageScore loans from approved lenders immediately, as an alternative to FICO. This effectively concludes the era in which FICO Classic was the only approved score in GSE mortgage world. The updated loan-level pricing adjustment will present a level playing field between FICO and VantageScore. Previously FICO bulls argued that the pricing grids would favor the classic FICO score and they are wrong. FICO's stock has been under assault with the stock down over 40% this year. Full disclosure, I have been short FICO for a few months. I flag this story because in a few weeks we will post an interview with a sell-side analyst who covers FICO and the credit bureaus and we are going to examine this story in great depth. Now let's move on to health insurance. It looks like that for now the industry is turning, or at least appearing to turn. With respect to UNH, we have spoken about UNH for many months now. The company has been dealing with high medical costs as well as problems in its Optum division, a division that provides medical services to Medicare Advantage customers. The bull case for UNH, the case for a turnaround, has been that UNH's traditional health insurance division is a short-tailed business. And that means that UNH has the ability to reprice fairly quickly. It looks like that's what happened this quarter. UNH reported earnings per share of $7.23 versus $6.57 expected. So a nice beat. But I would just point out that earnings per share was flat versus last year. Revenue though was also a little bit better than expected. The EPS beat was largely driven by improvement in the medical loss ratio, otherwise known as MLR. Also Optum Health margins while still weak were 5.4% versus expectations of 3.2%. Now UNH still has major issues with respect to Optum Health. As we've discussed before, Optum is an aggressive coder and the new Medicare Advantage healthcare pricing system penalizes companies like Optum. But that's a negative story that may unfold more in the future. For now, investors will look at the improvement in the medical loss ratio and hope for continued improvement. Investors love a turnaround story, especially where there is at least some evidence of a turnaround. That's why UNH has rallied so nicely. But investors have been disappointed in the turnaround stories of Starbucks and Nike. Investors keep hoping for evidence of a turnaround in those two companies, but that evidence remains elusive. Elevance also reported, but the results were not quite as positively received as UNH's. Earnings per share for the first quarter was a 5% growth beat, but was largely driven by non-recurring investment income. The company also added to claims reserves which should help 2027 earnings per share. Still, the consensus for 2026 remains for total EPS decline of 15%. But not bad. Molina. This is a medical insurance company mostly in the Medicaid space. Now during COVID, the population of Medicaid participants exploded. The government is now pruning the rolls, with the result that the remaining Medicaid participants are composed of sicker cohorts, thereby driving up the medical loss ratios of companies like Molina. Eventually, the government has to raise prices, but it does not seem to be in any rush. Now Molina reported this week. 1Q earnings per share beat by a nice 45 cents. So $2.35 is what they earned versus the $1.90 consensus, driven by an 80 basis points medical loss ratio beat, partially offset by somewhat higher SG&A. Despite the beat, the company only affirmed 2026 earnings guidance. But they do expect to update full-year guidance when they report second quarter. Now on the negative side, Medicaid membership attrition continued. Total Medicaid patients were 4.498 million in the quarter versus 4.568 million in the fourth quarter. And continued attrition means a potentially higher future medical loss ratio. Molina, like other medical insurance companies, has had a bounce off of the bottom. I would be careful with the Molina stock. Continued Medicaid attrition means that the remaining participants are part of sicker cohorts, and that does not bode well for future earnings. Now the current estimate for Molina for 2026 is $5, which is down 54% versus last year. Investors are playing for a turnaround and a big jump in earnings in 2027. I'm just not yet convinced. Moving on to industrials. Let's start with GE. Now GE has split into three companies: GE Aerospace, GE Vernova, and GE Healthcare. This week we will be discussing GE Aerospace which is symbol as the old GE, and GE Vernova symbol GEV. First GE. GE manufactures jet engines, largely for commercial airlines. And that business tends to go in waves. There are periods where airlines are expanding and need more planes, followed by periods of retrenchment. We are in a period without question of expansion right now. And that's why GE has been reporting good quarters, and it did so again. Earnings per share was $1.86 versus $1.49 last year, 25% growth, and a beat. Revenue beat as well and was up an very impressive 29%. Most importantly, total orders of 23 billion were up, get this, 87%. Yes, the war is causing investors to worry about travel-related companies, but so far airlines are not pulling back from buying new airplanes. GEV. Full disclosure, I've owned this stock for a while. Before we discuss earnings let me explain the basic story. GEV has three businesses. It manufactures gas turbines for utilities. It has an electrification equipment business and a wind turbine business. The wind business loses money. The key is the gas turbine and electrification equipment businesses and this is one of the best AI-related stories out there. Prior to 2020 electrical production in the US was barely growing. Thereafter, because of reindustrialization and the creation of AI data centers, electrical production is now growing around 3% per year. Now that may not sound like much, but 3% off of the huge electrical base of the United States, trust me, is an enormously large number. And we are constantly hearing about AI data centers and their need for electricity. And how they are looking at nuclear and any other alternatives they can get their hands on. Regardless, electrical production in the United States can only grow with the help of gas turbines. And gas turbines are manufactured by only three companies in the world: GEV, Siemens, and Mitsubishi. And that, in a nutshell, is the GEV story plus the benefits reaped by the electrification equipment division. Now onto earnings. GEV reported earnings per share of $3.30 versus $1.78, a beat, and up a whopping 85% versus last year. Revenue beat as well. Even more importantly, orders were 18.3 billion, up 71% year-over-year. And the company also raised guidance. These are very powerful numbers. Now the one caveat is that the 2026 PE is, get this, 80 times. However, this is a very, very long-tail business. Orders taken today do not create revenue until years from now. It takes that long to manufacture gas turbines. Thus, given the growth in orders, the company has earnings visibility for many years. Because of the strong growth, the 2028 PE is 36 times, which seems more reasonable. Boeing. This has been a very problematic company for years. But things seem to be moving in the right direction. The company reported a loss of 20 cents per share for the quarter versus a loss of 49 cents per share last year. The EPS estimate for 2026 is roughly zero. However, the stock rallied on this report because it reported lower-than-expected cash outflow of 1.45 billion. And it also delivered the most aircraft in the first quarter since 2019. Commercial aircraft deliveries rose to 143 planes. Moving on to Tesla. I have to say this is a funny stock. The investors who are short, and I'm not one of them, all point to how poorly the company is doing in its auto business. The investors who are long don't care and point to how well Tesla will do in the future in autonomous vehicles and robots. It's like the two sides are talking about entirely different companies. I would tread very carefully. Moving on to homebuilders. First DHI. Now homebuilders have been having a hard time. The year started with some hope as the 10-year yield declined below 4%. However, with the inflationary pressures created by the war, the 10-year got as high as 4.4%, thereby putting a real crimp into the all-important spring selling season. DHI results however were not terrible. Closings came in modestly below guidance but this was offset by slightly stronger gross margins. Company reported earnings per share of 2.24 versus 2.58 last year, so down 13%, but that was in line with expectations. And the company maintained its earnings per share guidance for 2026 of down around 10% versus 2025. In absolute terms these results are not great, but expectations for the sector are so low that DHI and the group rallied on these numbers. The hope is that the war will end and interest rates will come down. Meritage Homes reported, and here too the results were not great, but not terrible. Earnings per share of 82 cents was down 51% versus last year. That's bad. But orders declined only 5% versus last year, which is less bad. On the positive front, free cash flow was stronger than expected as the company has begun to dial back land spend in favor of increased share repurchases given the stock's below-tangible book value valuation. Meritage purchased 3% of shares outstanding during the quarter. Now I recommended Meritage in January and so far it has not worked out because the war caused rates to climb pretty quickly. I still like the stock because the company continues to grow tangible book value and the stock is valued at less than tangible book value of $75. Finally, Pulte Home also reported and here too the results were not great, but not a disaster. Earnings per share was $1.79, down 30% versus last year. But on the positive side, orders were actually up 3% versus last year. Clearly higher interest rates have hurt the spring selling season for the entire group. And now we turn to our two mailbags from two viewers. Our first question is from Canaan who asks: "Dear Steve, I've been an enthusiastic subscriber to the YouTube channel for a while, and I have learned an enormous amount through it, especially about macroeconomic trends and influential market forces that often don't make the front pages of mainstream newspapers. You've made me smarter and more financially capable, and I thank you for it. Financial news outlets have covered Hank Paulson's recent exhortation that the govt make a plan for a collapse in the T-note market, noting that a govt debt crisis could be far more impactful than crises in other markets. I try to avoid alarmism, but the 1BBB legislation has ballooned our projected deficit and severely impacted lower- and middle-class consumers at a time when White House foreign policy has also dealt blow after blow to America's global standing. Do you consider Paulson's warning well-founded, and if so, how might everyday investors/non-investing consumers position ourselves relative to it? All the best, Canaan." A very fair question. Ever since the great financial crisis there has been this weird competition to see who can predict the next financial calamity. And the latest competition has been the focus around private credit. Here Hank Paulson is focusing on the federal deficit and the high levels of debt-to-GDP in the United States. The federal debt-to-GDP level is at an all-time high of 125%, and some people are worried, like Paulson, that investors will just stop buying US Treasuries. Now Hank Paulson is chiming in on this one. But first of all what I would say is that with all due respect to former Secretary of the Treasury Paulson, he's not so good at predicting a crisis. On August 1, 2007, Paulson stated that "the market impact of the U.S. subprime mortgage fallout is largely contained and that the global economy is as strong as it has been in decades." Looking back, that's about as bad a prediction as you can get. But everyone makes mistakes, so let's focus on this prediction. It's not like Paulson is the first to make such a prediction. People have been predicting the same thing about the impact of the deficit for 40 years. 40 years is a long time to predict a calamity. Two points here. First, Japan's debt-to-GDP ratio is 240%, almost double the United States, and Japan's debt markets function quite well. Second, the price of this risk is interest rates. If the market was worried about the deficit, truly, truly worried, then the 10-year Treasury yield would be considerably higher. Instead it's been in a range of 3.9 to 4.5% for the last several years. The commentators of this calamity never seem to ask why their 40-year predictions have not occurred. And I think I have an answer for that. The global financial system functions on Treasuries. Banks park money overnight with each other through a multi-trillion dollar global repo market that only uses short-term Treasuries. When large institutions need to park significant money for any duration, they buy long-term Treasuries. Why? Because there is no alternative. At least not yet. If and when there is a large liquid alternative to Treasuries, I will become more worried about the deficit. Our second comment comes from Boris. This is a long email about the current debate surrounding software and I have edited his comments down for the sake of brevity. I thought Boris's comments were very interesting. And he starts with the following: "Thanks for the podcast - I've been listening for a while and I find it very useful. Quick background: I'm a computer scientist, spent ~10 years as a Product Manager at Google, then co-founded a startup in 2019 (acquired in 2022). Over the last 2 years, I went back to coding quite actively, because AI tools made it viable for me again. I use Cursor, Claude Code and Codere daily. I wanted to share a practitioner perspective on the recent AI / software sell-off (I do have positions in some names mentioned). Main Thesis: AI made software development much cheaper and more accessible - but people are over-extrapolating what that changes. B2C Software: B2C software is usually not that complex technically. The moats are elsewhere: network effects (WhatsApp, Reddit), marketplaces (Uber, DoorDash), content (YouTube, TikTok), brand. In B2C, AI coding unlocks a long tail of small products that were previously uneconomic. I'm already seeing many one-person businesses, but most won't scale into public companies. Megacaps will likely benefit the most, since user acquisition is the real bottleneck - my running joke is that VC money for B2C startups is going straight into Google and Meta pockets. B2B Software: 1. Internalization. There's a lot of talk about it, and I think it's overstated. Key constraints: TCO - building is easy, maintaining is not (patching vulnerabilities, updating dependencies, remaining compliant with regulatory changes, doing user support). Talent - AI helps a lot, but system design still matters. Many companies don't have people with sufficient skills and a required mindset. For example, many gyms use Exercise.com to run their business, and it's unlikely to be internalized. Risk - no CIO wants core systems like payments processing being "vibe-coded." Compliance - in regulated industries, software tools must pass audits and certification. Vendors amortize this cost across many customers; internal builds don't. Valid point though is that companies will require less seats due to automation, which undermines companies with per-seat pricing. I wonder how it will change going forward (units of work/pricing?). 2. Competition / New entrants. Lower dev costs cut both ways. Everyone benefits, not only newcomers. Incumbents can also ship faster, reduce development costs, and expand margins. It's not obvious this lower development cost structurally favors challengers. 3. Sales (this part is often missing in the discussion). For most software companies, the largest cost is sales & marketing, not R&D. Examples: Palantir - sales and marketing is ~49% of opex, Salesforce - 59%, Shopify - 41%, Amplitude - 55%, Elastic - 51%. AI-assisted coding doesn't really reduce sales costs, especially for large enterprise contracts. New entrants will face similar go-to-market expenses. Also, many large use cases are already captured by existing SaaS companies, so entry costs are structurally high. Agentic systems: they are very exciting for many reasons, but two issues are underweighted: agents quality evaluation, tuning and testing is hard and requires specialized set of skills, and their outputs are non-deterministic, i.e. the same input produces different results. That limits use in critical workflows (e.g. financial systems). I see this as an additional automation layer, not a full SaaS replacement. Small point on SBC: Under GAAP SBC is expensed at grant price, not market price. Example: grant of 1000 RSUs at $100 vesting evenly over 4 years results in GAAP expense $25k/year, even if the stock goes much higher in the meantime. Economically, the bigger issue for shareholders is dilution, not the accounting expense. Even there, grant sizes tend to adjust with stock price over time, since they're typically tied to cash compensation levels. Most companies disclose total outstanding SBC (shares/options) in their annual report notes. Curious how you think about this, especially the sales vs R&D angle. Regards, Boris." And here are my takeaways. The large incumbents I think, they'll survive this AI assault, but, and it's a big big big but, seat growth I think is going to slow, and the raising of prices that this industry has enjoyed for decades will just become much, much, much more difficult. So the long-term bull case of software was always increasing seats and prices, and I think a lot of that goes away or is questionable. As far as his comments on stock-based compensation, I just disagree. Yes, the companies of course disclose the stock-based comp. It is however a real cost. That is why GAAP accounting requires it to be expensed. The idea that you can pay someone in stock and not count that expense when you report earnings is to me just ridiculous. After all, all these companies deduct stock-based compensation from their taxes. They treat it as a real expense then. Howie Liu On How To Future-Proof Your Company

Forbes(2.1M) ·20:46 ·306 views · 2026-04-24
Howie Liu (Founder and CEO, Airtable)
> "When apocalypse is coming...
TL;DR: The current AI revolution is a multi-layered "SaaS apocalypse" happening at 10x speed, requiring leaders to abandon traditional management techniques in favor of hands-on technical immersion and "agentic" business models where tiny teams generate billion-dollar revenues. ...

TL;DR: The current AI revolution is a multi-layered "SaaS apocalypse" happening at 10x speed, requiring leaders to abandon traditional management techniques in favor of hands-on technical immersion and "agentic" business models where tiny teams generate billion-dollar revenues.

Fundraising and Financial Resilience

  • Capital Strategy: Airtable has raised $1.4 billion to date, maintaining over $1 billion on its balance sheet while generating $100 million in annual free cash flow.
  • Inflection Timing: Liu advises raising capital only during or immediately before a company inflection point. Raising during a "slog" is significantly more difficult and less favorable for founders.
  • Safety Cushions: A common mistake is raising only 6–12 months of runway. Liu recommends raising significantly more than needed to provide a buffer for errors and product iteration.
      • Example: In their first round, Airtable raised $3 million instead of $1 million to ensure they had several years of runway rather than just 12 months.
  • The "Money is Time" Philosophy: Capital should be viewed as a tool to buy the time necessary to reach the next major milestone without the pressure of imminent insolvency.

Navigating the "SaaS Apocalypse"

  • Multi-Layered Disruption: Liu argues that AI is not a single shift (like the move to mobile) but rather 3–4 simultaneous disruptions occurring at unprecedented speeds.
  • The Billion-Dollar Solo Founder: He agrees with Sam Altman’s prediction that agentic leverage will allow companies of 1–10 people to generate hundreds of millions or billions in revenue.
  • Structural Shifts: The very nature of how companies operate is changing; instead of visiting websites (e.g., Nike.com), customers will likely use agents to shop and interact with services.
  • Wartime Leadership: During this period of disruption, "business as usual" is a recipe for failure. Leaders must focus on winning the technological "war" rather than optimizing internal processes or engagement.

Airtable’s AI Pivot: From No-Code to Hyperagents

  • Product Evolution: Airtable began as a no-code app-building platform in 2013, moved into enterprise sales five years later, and is now returning to its roots as a Product-Led Growth (PLG) company powered by AI.
  • Omni AI: This is Airtable’s internal AI feature that allows users to build applications through natural language conversations rather than manual UI configuration.
  • Hyperagent: A new, separate product play from Airtable designed to allow users to build and deploy their own frontier-model agents (e.g., at the level of Claude/OpenAI) to run entire company operations.
  • Agentic Leverage: Liu emphasizes that agents are moving beyond "chat" to autonomous work, capable of completing tasks in 30 minutes that previously took humans days.

Strategic Advice for Executives

  • Stay at the "Edge": Liu asserts that a CEO's primary job today is staying current with AI developments. Traditional management tasks (one-on-ones, all-hands) are secondary to understanding the technological frontier.
  • Real-Time Information Sources:
      • X (formerly Twitter): Liu identifies X as the essential source for AI news, noting that major companies often release groundbreaking updates there via developer posts rather than formal press releases.
      • Technical Immersion: Leaders must personally experiment with tools like "Claude Code" or "Hyperagent" to develop the "vocabulary" of what is possible in an agentic world.
  • The Speed of Innovation: Traditional annual launch cycles (like Salesforce’s Dreamforce) are becoming obsolete because the technology changes too much within a single month to wait for a yearly event.
"When apocalypse is coming... you don't maintain business as usual. What matters is winning or losing the war. And I think the only way to do this in this moment is to actually get hands-on with the most frontier AI experience that you can." — Howie Liu
"I think it's going to be the default that you're going to have very, very small teams... that build enormously leveraged companies that generate hundreds of millions if not billions of revenue because they're able to be fully agentically leveraged." — Howie Liu
"If you do nothing else as a CEO except to go and stay focused on what's happening every single week and constantly be playing around with it... you couldn't do much better than that." — Howie Liu

Every other time is a horrible time to raise because if you're slogging it out and there's not like a, you know, kind of a light at the end of the tunnel, you're not going to be able to convince investors to come in right then. So Fortune 500, we're going to do these big multi-million dollar contracts, get entire pockets of the org on the product. That looks very different as a business, right? Requires a different type of team, you know, leadership, approach to building product and go-to-market. And we're now actually kind of in a little bit of a pendulum swinging back to our roots, which is I think in the era of AI, the best products in my opinion in AI, whether it's ChatGPT or Claude or some of the newer products like Lovable, Replit, etc., they're all working because they're just great products and people love using them and they come in droves, right? It no longer takes many, many years to hit 100 million in revenue with the best AI products. If you can create a self-serve motion where people just experience the magic of your product immediately, you get really, really fast adoption. So we're also now kind of shifting back into this gear of how do we make just really great products powered by AI that are immediately valuable to people who come in and try it. SpaceX-Cursor Deal, SaaS Debt Bomb, New Apple CEO, SPLC Indictment, Colon Cancer Spike

All-In Podcast(1.1M) ·90:42 ·331.1K views · 2026-04-24
Chamath Palihapitiya (Co-host, All-In Podcast) Jason Calacanis (Co-host, All-In Podcast) David Sacks (Co-host, All-In Podcast)
> "Debt equals prison bitch.
TL;DR: SpaceX’s $60 billion agreement to acquire AI coding startup Cursor marks a major shift toward vertical integration in the AI arms race, while a "SaaS bloodbath" unfolds as AI-driven deflation and high debt levels crush legacy software valuations. The Deal Terms: SpaceX...

TL;DR: SpaceX’s $60 billion agreement to acquire AI coding startup Cursor marks a major shift toward vertical integration in the AI arms race, while a "SaaS bloodbath" unfolds as AI-driven deflation and high debt levels crush legacy software valuations.

SpaceX-Cursor Deal: Compute as Strategic Leverage

  • The Deal Terms: SpaceX has reached an agreement to acquire Cursor for $60B by the end of 2026, or pay a $10B "breakup fee" for their collaboration.
      • Valuation Logic: Cursor is currently at a $2B run rate, expected to hit $6B by the end of 2026. At 30x revenue, the deal is considered accretive for SpaceX as it targets a $2T IPO valuation.
      • Strategic Fit: The acquisition merges Cursor’s elite coding IDE with xAI’s foundation models and SpaceX’s massive compute infrastructure (the Colossus cluster).
  • Vertical Integration: David Sacks noted that Cursor was previously dependent on foundation model companies (OpenAI, Anthropic) that were beginning to compete directly with them. This deal provides Cursor with independent compute and a dedicated model.
  • CEO Elon Musk’s Vision: Musk recently stated that xAI is being rebuilt from the "foundations up." Integrating Cursor provides the training data and enterprise client base needed to move to the front of the coding AI leaderboard within 12 months.

The SaaS "Debt Bomb" and AI Deflation

  • Medallia’s Collapse: Private equity firm Thoma Bravo is reportedly handing the keys of portfolio company Medallia back to creditors, wiping out ~$5B in equity.
      • Predictability Crisis: Medallia’s debt servicing costs reportedly tripled (from $100M to $300M) while the sales team missed targets significantly.
      • The "SaaS-pocalypse": Chamath Palihapitiya argued that vertical SaaS is being crushed because enterprises can now "spin up agents" to perform custom workflows for a fraction of the cost of a seat-based license.
  • Deflationary Pressure: AI is lowering the "unit cost" of software. David Friedberg highlighted that AI acts as a deflationary force on business operations, which impairs the debt used to finance legacy SaaS buyouts that relied on 120% net dollar retention.
  • The "Founder Index": Sacks suggested that in this era of rapid disruption, investors should favor companies still led by founders. Founders are willing to "burn the boats" and reinvent their business models, whereas hired managers tend to protect legacy fee structures (e.g., Workday’s AI toll vs. Salesforce’s "headless" agent approach).

Apple’s Leadership Transition: The Ternus Era

  • The Succession: John Ternus, a 25-year hardware veteran, is set to succeed Tim Cook as CEO.
  • Tim Cook’s Legacy: The Besties characterized Cook as the "ultimate steward" rather than an innovator.
      • Financial Performance: Under Cook, Apple’s market cap grew 10x, revenue grew from $100B to $400B, and the company reduced its share count by 44%.
      • The Innovation Gap: Jason Calacanis argued that Apple missed major swings in search, self-driving cars, and high-functioning AI (Siri), focusing instead on "wringing profits" from Steve Jobs’ original product lines.
  • The "Michael Jordan" Problem: Chamath noted that Cook had the impossible task of following a legendary founder. Ternus now faces the challenge of a "heterogeneous world" where AI may decay the moats of single-device ecosystems (iPhone).

Government Efficiency and Civil Discourse

  • DOGE and Waste: The group discussed the "racism industrial complex" and "homeless industrial complex" in California, praising citizen journalists like Nick Shirley for uncovering government fraud.
  • Political Sentiment: Sacks reported on a recent meeting with President Trump, describing him as "genial" and "interested in the subject matter," specifically praising his "behind the meter" power generation strategy for AI data centers.

Yeah, so the underlying problem is that these businesses in the SaaS space, where you're driven by net new sales every year, how many new customers are you signing up and then you're trying to manage retention and you're trying to increase sell-through and retain customers. They're just having a really hard time sourcing new customers and there's probably higher than modeled attrition. That's right. And when you have a very kind of typically historically predictable business where you can say, "Hey, I've got a net revenue retention of 118%" or what have you, meaning I'm—I'm selling into my installed base by 18% over what I'm making last year and then I'm signing up new customers. You can leverage that business, right? You can borrow money against those cash flows because it becomes predictable. And what's happened in the last year, in particular, is agents have become so good and so fast and so cheap that many enterprises can simply spin up an alternative to a vertical SaaS solution and that's crushing the sales team's ability to sell in. That's who you're competing against. Now, I want to make one point and just link this with something else that happened this week, and that's Kevin Warsh's hearing for Fed Reserve Chair. Kevin Warsh went and talked a lot about the deflationary effect of AI. And I actually think we all talk about the SaaS-pocalypse as if it's this sort of like isolated business phenomenon where these SaaS companies are getting blown up. I think another lens to look at what's going on is the incredible deflation of how much it costs to successfully run a business and you don't have to pay a premium price for SaaS products anymore, meaning that that piece of the business can suddenly get much cheaper. AI is delivering on its deflationary promise. I'll just say one thing about what Warsh said. Warsh spoke a lot about the deflationary evolution promised by AI and that he expects that it will drive productivity growth like we've never seen before. But he said, "I don't know what that's going to do to the job market." That there may be a dislocation between that productivity growth being realized and how the labor markets are going to be able to respond to those changes. But fundamentally, he's saying that we're going to see economic deflation. The problem with economic deflation is that when it occurs, it means some business is seeing their revenue go down. And if that segment of the economy is leveraged, if they have debt sitting on top of that piece of the economy where it's supposed to always, always, always grow—like a SaaS company's top line is always supposed to grow—suddenly that debt gets impaired. And that can have an economic ripple effect that is adverse. But what he's pointing out is that as a result of deflation—because it's not coming from some cost-cutting or economic contraction—what he's saying is that the deflationary forces ultimately lead to economic expansion because other parts of the economy will now grow. So if I can suddenly cut, you know, call it 50% of my SaaS budget and I can reinvest that capital in other ways of growing my business instead of managing my expenses, all of a sudden my enterprise will grow and the economy will grow. He also said, just as an aside and I want to make sure I cover this so—so that we're really clear, he said the way that we've been measuring inflation is wrong and that he doesn't agree with the way the Fed has been measuring inflation because you can do a survey of any household and they'll tell you, "My God, everything's so expensive." So all of the indices and bullshit that are being used to calculate an inflation index is completely misrepresenting what the average American is actually feeling. And so he wants to rethink how the Fed is addressing inflation from an interest rate perspective, but he does think that the overall kind of economic picture is one of deflationary pressure and productivity gains coming out of AI. Sacks, I'll—I'll drop this off to you. I think it's pretty clear what's happening here is that the loss—SaaS's loss is the token dealer's gain, right? So what is the role of venture capital and then private equity in addressing the software market? Software was eating the world, now tokens are eating—the SaaS business and the software business, yeah? Well, I'm of two minds about this. I'm—I'm going to talk about the opportunity for—for private equity. Let me just say, backing up, that historically we only had two good exits for software businesses. One was IPO, the other was M&A. And then these big private equity shops came along and gave us a third potential exit, which is you would sell to them and then they would raise the capital based on, I don't know, one-third equity and two-thirds debt. So it was debt-financed buyouts, which is something that's been around in let's call it the non-tech part of the economy for a long time but was a relatively new entrant into the world of technology. And the reason for that is that if you're going to debt-finance a purchase, you need to have very stable cash flows, because if you miss, if your cash flows miss and you can't pay your interest on the debt, then you're going to lose all your equity because the debt holders will foreclose. So, in order to do a debt-financing of any kind, you have to have very predictable cash flows and it was believed for a long time that software did have those predictable cash flows. At least for the mature businesses, the ones that were at the stage where they could IPO as a potential alternative. So, it was a very attractive thing, I think, like I said, it was great to have that third option. I'm—I'm of two minds about where the private equity business is today. On the one hand, the pricing now has got to be super attractive for them. I mean, we're seeing public SaaS companies that are doing a billion of ARR... They're free. They're free. With 20% growth rates, 80% gross margins, and they're trading at three times ARR. You know, you can buy a dollar for 50 cents. So is—is that an opportunity, Sacks, you think? Do you think we've hit rock bottom? We should do a rollup. Well, on the one hand, I do think that the pricing has never been more attractive if you're a private equity shop looking at a business like that. I mean, those companies used to be valued at 13 times ARR, now it's three. And I'm talking about like a category leader. By the way, Salesforce is off 9% today. I don't know if you guys saw this, but the market's absolutely tanking today after the—the Medallia announcement came out. Right, okay. So—so that would be like—you know, I said I'm of two minds about it. So I would be bullish for private equity just based on pricing, but the bearish part is that in order for their business model to work, you have to have predictable cash flows. You can't have a SaaS company go from, I don't know, 120% net dollar retention one quarter to 80% net dollar retention six months or a year later because a big part of their customer base has attritted to using tokens, right? Or to basically creating some bespoke software. You just said the absolute critical thing in all of this, which is you have to have predictable cash flows. I think what happens is when you're a startup, you typically have to figure out how to disruptively price to enter the market. So you're like, "Okay, if I deliver $10 of value, I'm going to charge a dollar." And that's—that's the normal playbook, like a 10% ratio, right, of price to value. The problem is when you start to stack venture capital into it and you then stack growth equity into it, what you're effectively creating in—in the preference stack of your company is that you are creating a higher return hurdle. Right? You've got to clear 300 million, 500 million, a billion of pref, and then you have to return 15 or 20% on top of that. So what do people do as they raise more money? They increase price. But the problem is at some point when you increase price, you engender a ton of competition and you put a huge target on your back. Private equity is the last stop, because when they come in and they layer in billions and billions of dollars of not just equity but also debt, and that has to then be completely predictable and paid back, their only lever is to raise price. They can never cut price to take share. They don't—they can't underwrite that to pay back their debt holders. And so, Sacks, part of the big problem here and why nobody wants to touch these companies is that they are overpriced. Yes, they're making a billion dollars of ARR, but the unit cost has gotten out of control. It used to be 10% of value. It's probably now 30% of value, and everybody's looking at their contracts thinking, "Well, when it comes time to a renewal, I'm going to just cut this in half," or "I'm going to cut this by two-thirds," or "I'm going to cut this by 75%" because the value isn't there anymore. Or they can threaten to and negotiate a better deal. And it becomes even worse because the minute you make these products headless, right?
