Budget Hearing – Department of Commerce

House Appropriations Committee(12.1K) ·84:01 ·2.4K views · 2026-04-24
Secretary of Commerce Howard Lutnick defended a $9.2 billion FY 2027 budget request—a 16.5% decrease from current levels—focusing on "America First" trade policies and semiconductor reshoring while facing intense scrutiny over major program eliminati...
TL;DR Secretary of Commerce Howard Lutnick defended a $9.2 billion FY 2027 budget request—a 16.5% decrease from current levels—focusing on "America First" trade policies and semiconductor reshoring while facing intense scrutiny over major program eliminations, tariff-induced...

TL;DR

Secretary of Commerce Howard Lutnick defended a $9.2 billion FY 2027 budget request—a 16.5% decrease from current levels—focusing on "America First" trade policies and semiconductor reshoring while facing intense scrutiny over major program eliminations, tariff-induced inflation, and personal ethics.

FY 2027 Budget Priorities and Domestic Program Cuts

  • Strategic Spending Reductions: The $9.2 billion request represents a $1.8 billion decrease, achieved primarily by eliminating long-standing economic development programs.
      • Elimination of EDA and MBDA: The administration proposes zeroing out the Economic Development Administration (EDA) and the Minority Business Development Agency (MBDA).
          • Secretary Lutnick cited "brutal" Inspector General reports claiming 92% of MBDA awards were flawed and describing the programs as "broken."
      • Manufacturing Extension Partnership (MEP): The budget proposes eliminating this program, which helps small and medium-sized manufacturers.
  • Broadband Savings: Secretary Lutnick highlighted a $21 billion "saving" in the Broadband Equity Access and Deployment (BEAD) program.
      • Technology Neutrality: By removing "fiber-only" requirements and allowing satellite and fixed wireless options, the department claims to have met initial goals for $21 billion instead of the appropriated $42 billion.
  • Administrative Delays: Ranking Member Grace Meng and Chair Hal Rogers noted that spend plans and budget documents were submitted weeks late, with the department failing to answer many written questions from the previous year.

"America First" Trade and Manufacturing Reshoring

  • Semiconductor Investments: Lutnick touted a "golden age" of manufacturing, citing massive domestic investments:
      • Micron: $200 billion for memory semiconductors in Idaho and New York.
      • TSMC: $165 billion for advanced logic manufacturing in Arizona.
      • Texas Instruments: $60 billion for seven fabs in Texas and Utah.
  • Trade Deficit and Tariffs: The administration reported that 2025 saw the lowest trade deficit in 16 years, with exports rising 6% to $3.4 trillion.
      • Tariff Criticism: Ranking Member Rosa DeLauro countered that tariffs cost American families an average of $1,700 in 2025 and led to a net loss of 89,000 manufacturing jobs between Feb 2025 and Feb 2026.
      • Refund Controversy: Following a Supreme Court ruling declaring certain tariffs illegal, the department is overseeing a refund process. Rep. Madeleine Dean (D-PA) alleged the Secretary’s family members were "profiteering" by buying refund rights.
  • Energy Projects: The department is prioritizing large-scale natural gas and coal investments, including a 9.2-gigawatt natural gas facility in Ohio and new coal mines in Pennsylvania.

National Security and Technology Protection

  • Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) Expansion: The budget seeks a $215 million increase for BIS to nearly double the size of its annual appropriation.
      • Enforcement Personnel: The funds will hire hundreds of special agents to prevent the "theft of American technological leadership" by China and other bad actors.
      • Revenue Generation: Lutnick argued these agents "pay for themselves," generating 3 to 10 times their cost in fines against companies violating export controls.
  • Global Trade Deals: The Secretary highlighted a $550 billion trade deal with Japan and new pharmaceutical agreements with the EU intended to lower domestic drug prices through "most favored nation" status.

Ethics, Oversight, and Management Concerns

  • Jeffrey Epstein Allegations: Reps. Meng and Dean questioned Lutnick regarding his relationship with Jeffrey Epstein.
      • Contradictory Statements: Members cited documents showing a 2012 visit to Epstein's private island and a 2018 business deal (Adfin) involving Epstein, despite Lutnick’s previous claims of cutting ties in 2005.
      • Response: Lutnick declined to answer specific questions, deferring to a planned voluntary meeting with the House Oversight Committee.
  • National Weather Service (NOAA): The budget includes $135 million for shipbuilding and unmanned systems.
      • Modernization: Lutnick stated the agency is moving from "hardwired" technology to the cloud to improve tornado and hurricane forecasting.
      • Staffing Levels: Democratic members challenged Lutnick’s previous assertions that no meteorologists were fired, citing ongoing vacancies in front-line positions.
"The Department of Commerce is the front line and our key defense from China trying to get our best chips and our best technology... these agents bring between three and 10 times their revenue annually in fines."

— Secretary Howard Lutnick

"The President's trade war increased the cost of food for American families by $1,700 throughout 2025... [The administration] has had the worst year for job growth since 2020."

— Rep. Rosa DeLauro

"Never in modern US history has the Office of the Secretary of Commerce intersected so broadly and deeply with the financial interests of the Commerce Secretary’s own family."

— Rep. Grace Meng

Good morning. The subcommittee will be in order. Without objection, the Chair is authorized to declare a recess at any time. I would like to welcome everyone to this hearing. Thank you to our subcommittee members and to Secretary Lutnick for joining us to discuss the Department of Commerce fiscal year 2027 budget request. The request for the Department of Commerce totals $9.2 billion, a $1.8 billion or 16.5% decrease from the current level. The request reiterates and builds on the budget the President submitted to Congress last year. He seeks to reinforce and expand on efforts to counter the strong-arm trade tactics of China and refocuses the department's trade activities on investments that create jobs in America and protect national security. The Department of Commerce has a wide-ranging portfolio. It must level an international trade landscape tilted against America, foster economic growth, conduct weather research and response, and produce statistics about our nation and the economy. These responsibilities are bound together by the common goal of ensuring American prosperity. Its investments and resources are invaluable to rural communities like my district in Eastern Kentucky. From NOAA's weather forecasters who provide severe weather warnings when seconds count, to EDA's essential work to grow economic opportunity and the BEAD program's expansion of high-speed, reliable broadband to rural areas, opening opportunities and improving lives. The department's vital mission requires strong leadership and strong oversight. I would like to note that the committee only just this week received the department's complete fiscal year 2026 spend plans and detailed budget request documents for fiscal year 2027. Additionally, the department must ensure response to written questions from subcommittee members. Secretary Lutnick, these areas of communication and responsiveness should be a priority of yours. Over the past few months alone, the Department of Commerce has announced multiple $15 billion plus record-setting critical energy investments across this country. From Pennsylvania and Georgia to Texas and Tennessee, they will accelerate economic growth, ensure US energy dominance, bolster critical supply chains, and strengthen US national and economic security. In addition to the massive historic trade agreements with Korea and Taiwan, the impressive $550 billion trade deal with Japan has announced six projects in the past six months, which include $40 billion for small modular reactors in Tennessee and Alabama, a $33 billion 9.2 gigawatt natural gas-fired facility in Ohio, a $17 billion natural gas-fired power generation hub in Southwest Pennsylvania, and $16 billion for natural gas in East Texas. That goes on top of $2 billion for a deep-water crude oil export terminal in the Gulf of America and $600 million for a critical high-pressure high-temperature synthetic diamond grit facility in Georgia. Just last month, Secretary Wright and I were in Southern Ohio to break ground on that 9.2 gigawatt natural gas facility. This project will develop the largest natural gas facility in history. It'll lower electricity costs across the region, create thousands and thousands of American jobs, strengthen our national security, and help the US meet the energy needs of our future. Beyond these historic investments, the Department of Commerce continues to work around the clock to implement President Trump's America First trade policy. Under Section 232, the Commerce Department is taking action on steel, aluminum, copper, pharmaceuticals, autos, semiconductors, and investigating other sectors to protect, reshore, and revive manufacturing. These actions have led to multi-billion dollar commitments to reshore steel and aluminum manufacturing and restore American excellence in critical industries. In the first year of his second term, President Trump has dramatically reduced the trade deficit, lowering imports and increasing exports. Exports went to $3.4 trillion, which is a 6% increase from 2024. And in 2025, the trade deficit was the lowest we've seen in 16 years. I'm proud of the role I played in negotiating our most important trade agreements, including the largest deal ever done, which was our trade deal and investment deal with the European Union. BIS is working hand in hand with the Department of Health and Human Services and we've arranged historic agreements to lower drug prices for American consumers, negotiating most favored nation deals and ensuring Americans pay the lowest prices for innovative medicines. As a result of these deals, additionally, we have secured over $400 billion in commitments to reshore pharmaceuticals and manufacturing in America, those key pharmaceuticals. The budget includes a $215 million increase to BIS above the 2026 enacted level, which supports the hiring of hundreds of new special agent law enforcement officials to address the theft of American technological leadership by bad actors. These officers are critical to protecting and defending America's innovation. In ITA we are driving record foreign direct investment, cracking down on harmful foreign trade practices, and supporting our 1.3 million American jobs with export and investment deals. In 2025, ITA helped Boeing, Wabtec and other major American companies secure a record $244 billion in contracts with foreign public buyers. This exceeds the 17 billion that the Biden administration did in its first year. The budget includes a $10 million increase to the 2026 enacted level for trade enforcement in ITA and $100 million for the United States Investment Accelerator. The budget slashes unnecessary wasteful spending at NIST and other groups, and eliminates the Manufacturing Extension Partnership, which I'm sure we will talk about. At the USPTO, we are rapidly modernizing and leveraging technology to cut classification prep dramatically. We want to get rid of the massive backlog that we had and do a much better job. And finally we have launched a new era of modernization of NOAA, where we are in the process of transferring the agency's IT system from a decades-old hardwired technology to the modern distributed cloud. This update will improve our forecasting capabilities, transform the way our forecasters do their jobs, and will make Americans safer. The budget also includes a $135 million increase to the 2026 enacted level for NOAA shipbuilding and our improvement of unmanned systems programs. For far too long our nation was mired in status quo, leaving American consumers and producers alike with unfair, poorly implemented trade deals, outsourced jobs, and paltry investments that failed to meet our country's potential. President Trump is committed to ushering in a golden age of domestic commerce and bringing jobs back to the United States and that's exactly what he's done in his first 15 months of this administration. Ross Gerber on Elon Musk & Tesla’s 16% Revenue Growth, Google Cloud Next Conference, AI Insurance

The Information(12.7K) ·35:54 ·854 views · 2026-04-24
Ross Gerber (President and CEO, Gerber Kawasaki) Akash Pasricha (Host, The Information) Elon Musk (CEO, Tesla)
Tesla faces scrutiny over narrow profitability and CEO Elon Musk's overextension, while the broader AI sector grapples with an "implementation gap" as corporations struggle to integrate agents and insurers move to exclude AI-related liabilities.
TL;DR Tesla faces scrutiny over narrow profitability and CEO Elon Musk's overextension, while the broader AI sector grapples with an "implementation gap" as corporations struggle to integrate agents and insurers move to exclude AI-related liabilities. Financial Performance:...

TL;DR

Tesla faces scrutiny over narrow profitability and CEO Elon Musk's overextension, while the broader AI sector grapples with an "implementation gap" as corporations struggle to integrate agents and insurers move to exclude AI-related liabilities.

Tesla’s Q1 Financials and the "Musk Factor"

  • Financial Performance: Despite a 16% jump in revenue, Tesla reported GAAP earnings of only 13 cents per share.
      • "Funny Business" in Numbers: Ross Gerber, CEO of Gerber Kawasaki, noted discrepancies between cash flow ($1.4 billion) and actual cash on the books ($700 million), suggesting that adjustments for FX, tariffs, and Musk’s massive compensation package are masking a lack of true profitability.
      • SEC Disclosures: A "hidden" $2 billion deal with an unnamed AI hardware company was discovered in SEC filings, highlighting Musk's massive capital investment in future compute.
  • CEO Tone and Focus: Musk struck a "downbeat" tone on the earnings call, contrasting sharply with the optimism of 2021.
      • Overextension Concerns: Gerber argues Musk has too many "irons in the fire"—including Tesla, SpaceX, xAI, and the Optimus robot—leading to a lack of focus that creates significant "Titanic-level" risk for investors.
      • Strategic Vision: Musk is currently building a "Terafab" factory for chips, solving for a projected compute shortage five to ten years away.

Google Cloud Next: The Rise of "Agent Sprawl"

  • Major Announcements: Google focused heavily on AI agents and unveiled new Tensor Processing Units (TPUs) specifically bifurcated for training versus inference.
  • The Implementation Gap: Reporting from Erin Woo suggests that AI lab innovation is currently outpacing corporate ability to implement it.
      • Bottlenecks: Companies like Home Depot report that while individual steps (like coding or ad copy) are 50% faster, the overall business workflow remains stalled by human-centric processes like market research and internal discussions.
      • Agent Sprawl: Customers are struggling with "agent sprawl," where various employees set up uncoordinated AI agents, leading to management and data security difficulties.
  • Competitive Landscape: Microsoft’s Copilot remains a dominant force due to its integration with the Office suite, though customers are increasingly looking to diversify with Google’s Gemini and Anthropic’s models.

ServiceNow and the Software Sell-off

  • Market Overreaction: ServiceNow reported 22% revenue growth but saw its stock drop 15%.
      • Profitability Guidance: The dip was attributed to lowered profitability guidance following the acquisition of cybersecurity firm Armis and slight subscription delays in the Middle East.
  • Strategic Pivot: ServiceNow is attempting to transition from a traditional software provider to a central "dashboard" for managing and safeguarding AI agents.

Exclusive: The Emergence of AI Insurance Exclusions

  • Liability Shifts: Major insurers are taking initial steps to file for "AI exclusions" in general liability policies, according to reporting by Laura Bratton.
      • Covered Risks: Exclusions target "advertising injuries" (such as copyright infringement from AI-generated ads) and potential bodily injury or property damage caused by AI-enabled hardware (e.g., malfunctioning robots).
      • Policy Segmentation: Insurers are signaling that AI-related losses should not be covered under traditional policies but rather through specialized, capped standalone cyber or "errors and omissions" policies.

The Case for a Tesla-SpaceX Merger

  • Conflict of Interest: Ross Gerber argues that a merger between Tesla and SpaceX is "inevitable" to resolve the legal and financial conflicts inherent in Musk running two highly intertwined companies.
  • The "Berkshire of AI": A combined entity would allow investors a "pure play" bet on Musk’s entire technology and space empire, though it would carry high "key man risk" regarding Musk’s health and longevity.

Notable Quotes

Will Social Media Bans Actually Work? | Bloomberg Tech: Asia 4/24/2026

Bloomberg Technology(721.0K) ·22:59 ·566 views · 2026-04-24
Shery Ahn (Host, Bloomberg) Win Gatchalian (Senator, Philippines) Ravi Iyer (Social Psychologist)
Governments worldwide are shifting toward hardline age-based restrictions for social media to combat youth mental health crises and platform addiction, moving beyond parental controls to treat digital access with the same regulatory weight as alcohol and tobacco.
TL;DR Governments worldwide are shifting toward hardline age-based restrictions for social media to combat youth mental health crises and platform addiction, moving beyond parental controls to treat digital access with the same regulatory weight as alcohol and tobacco. The...

TL;DR

Governments worldwide are shifting toward hardline age-based restrictions for social media to combat youth mental health crises and platform addiction, moving beyond parental controls to treat digital access with the same regulatory weight as alcohol and tobacco.

The Global Momentum for Youth Access Bans

  • The Australian Model: Australia has pioneered legislation requiring social media companies to keep users under 16 off their platforms, treating digital access as a regulated substance.
      • Early data suggests success; Snapchat usage among targeted groups dropped from 34% to 20% following enforcement.
  • Regional Expansion: Inspired by Australia, countries including Indonesia and Greece are initiating similar under-16 regulations. In Southeast Asia, Malaysia and the Philippines are the most active in considering legislative bans.
  • Legal Shifts in the U.S.: Beyond simple age limits, U.S. states are pursuing litigation based on "negligent design." Courts are increasingly viewing addictive algorithms as a product liability issue rather than a protected free speech issue.

Addressing the "Digital Addiction" Crisis

  • Senator Win Gatchalian (Philippines): Proposing a total ban for children under 16, citing data that Filipino youth spend an average of 5–6 hours daily on social media.
      • Impact on Well-being: Gatchalian argues that "doomscrolling" and late-night usage deprive youth of sleep and replace essential physical socialization with weaker digital bonds.
      • Constitutional Justification: While critics cite free speech, Gatchalian maintains that protecting the mental health and well-being of the nation’s youth provides a legal basis for limiting access.
  • Ravi Iyer (USC Marshall School): Argues that social media functions via "displacement," where 45% of U.S. children admit it interferes with sleep and 40% say it impacts school activities.
      • Gamified Interactions: Platforms use features like "Snapchat streaks" to psychologically compel usage, making it difficult for children to disconnect even when they recognize the negative impact.
      • Account vs. Content: Iyer clarifies that these measures are "age limits" rather than "bans," allowing youth to view content (like YouTube or TikTok videos) while preventing the creation of addictive, data-driven accounts.

Limits of Enforcement and Algorithmic Design

  • Catherine Thorbecke (Bloomberg Opinion): Expresses skepticism that age limits alone can solve the problem, noting that tech-savvy youth often find workarounds.
      • The Root Cause: Thorbecke argues that "somebody’s birthday" isn't what makes a platform dangerous; rather, it is the platform design, such as "rage bait" and algorithms optimized for maximum outrage and engagement.
      • The "Big Tobacco" Moment: Regulators are now viewing big tech through the lens of historical public health battles, suggesting that the current momentum is the best opportunity in a decade to force comprehensive safety reforms.
  • Implementation Hurdles:
      • Tech Compliance: Major platforms are currently under investigation for non-compliance with existing restrictions.
      • Parental Fatigue: Experts agree that parents are too exhausted to manage controls across dozens of apps, necessitating government intervention to change industry norms.

We're focusing today on the push to use regulation to protect young people from potential harms posed by social media. Australia has become the trailblazer for curbing youth access to digital platforms, treating the issue like alcohol or cigarettes. And it's an idea that's been gaining traction in other countries too. Social media giants are facing a reckoning as moves to curb its negative impact, especially on children, gain momentum around the world. In the US, Meta is facing mounting legal challenges over allegations that its social media platforms were created to be addictive. After a Los Angeles jury found Meta and Google negligent in the design and operation of their popular platforms in March, more US states are now discussing ways to limit young people on social media. Australia is leading the global push on that front. Last year, it forced social media giants including Meta, TikTok, Snap and X to keep users under 16 off their platforms. The global community is looking at Australia and saying, "Well, if Australia can do it, why can't we?" It's a profound reform which will continue to reverberate around the world. The Indonesian government has also initiated regulations aimed at removing under-16s from social media platforms. Greece will be doing something similar by the start of next year. In Southeast Asia, the Philippines and Malaysia appear to be the most active in considering similar measures to Indonesia's ban. And the movement is gaining global traction as more countries debate similar measures. The fate of the Australian experiment will provide important information to governments seeking to regulate social media. It's already encountering substantial implementation hurdles. Major tech platforms are under investigation for non-compliance, and reports say the restrictions are relatively easy for children to get around. There are also plenty of legal hurdles.
